RATING: 7.5
I've realized I just don't like these hide-and-seek games. Dracula is just awful as a prime example, but mainly that was due to FFG's over design of combat and stupid filigree of Arkham Horror-like items and systems. Blah! But then Whitechapel comes along with a stream-lined version of the system: you're just trying to find Jack and he's just trying to get away. The set of nights are all a build up to the final night - where Jack will be extremely hard pressed to kill and escape. Love the tension and the wow production. I can't imagine a better version of hide-an-seek. But gain, I just don't think I like these games, so this is probably as good as it gets for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
An absolutely wonderful deduction game, at least for two players. The fact that you need a little luck to find the blood trail and the complex layout of the map keep things interesting. The options are so many and the situation is ever evolving, which precludes you from experiencing that tedious phase of the typical deduction game when you're basically just trying to find the question that eliminates the most dead ends. In practice, frustration and analysis paralysis won't take over you during playing Letters from Whitechapel because not coming up with the most effective question does not make you feel stupid or having lost the game.

The game is interesting for both, the police and Jack players. There is a thick layer of metagaming which helps with immersion immensely. It is not just a question of mechanically converging towards the solution, but you actually have to guess what your opponent is up to. This is something that makes the game more authentic. You can actually feel the heat of the chase, try to pull off the wittiest of bluffs or just simply panic.

A great game truly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.95
Excellent deduction game that oozes with theme.  Excellent suspense and surprise, provided you don't introduce a lot of the house variants that are swimming around which more or less reduce the whole game to putting together a puzzle (detectives making copious notes in notebooks, marking off all possible positions of Jack on laminated mini-versions of the map, etc.).  

The major drawback of this game is its length (primarily a problem if more than one player are splitting the role of the detectives).  For what can be a 2-3 hour time investment, I'd normally like a game to be a little more meaty.  Works great (and plays more quickly) as a two-player game (at the cost that a single mental error on the part of the detectives may not be noticed immediately and may effectively ruin the chances of catching Jack).

RATING: 7
Letters from Whitechapel is an interesting game and what I enjoy the most about it is the historical feel that it has. The map is a close replica of the real Whitechapel, the spots where the wretched are placed are the places where the real victims were murdered, the policemen are exactly those who chased Jack... and so on. Also the visual impact of the game, while not outstanding, is still very intriguing.

As far as gameplay is concerned, the mechanics on their own are very simple, but it's so fascinating to engage in this deduction challenge with all the outguessing and all the tactics involved. This brings me to my only concern about the game. The depth and the enjoyment that Letters from Whitechapel can offer really depends on the people you're playing with. If either side is playing poorly, it gets too easy for the other and immediately the game feels flat... and the repetitive nature of the mechanics will not compensate for it at all. 

Letters from Whitechapel doesn't offer lots of strategies or dynamic gameplay, but it provides a very tense experience. It's particularly good with 2 players and it can be very challenging when everyone is familiar with it, in which case it's overall MUCH better. It's a very good game, but after 14 plays I just don't feel that itch to play it anymore. It's not a game I want to hang on to, especially because I want to keep my game collection within the borders that reason dictates.

RATING: 5
2-6(2)

Okay, so the Mr. Jack games aren't deduction games, and then I suppose this one is. I suppose.

I was hoping that this would capture the cat and mouse chase and the deductive elements and everything that I don't usually see in a board game.

This one's been tough so far. It's a bit dry, as all of the moves and actions are just pretty straightforward, with only a few "special powers", which are just shortcuts of a form.

I suppose that this should make the deductive part of the game more compelling, but it feels like a really fragile system.

It's easy for Jack to game the various key spots to make the chase much less interesting. It's pretty easy to lose the trail to make a whole day fruitless. And etc.

So far there hasn't really been any particularly interesting play, but I guess that's the hazard for a game that requires so much fuzzy thought as this (as opposed to some quintessential, perfectly balanced Euro, or something).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Letters is basically a refinement of Scotland Yard, a game I once owned and ultimately didn't enjoy very much.  Recasting Mr X as Jack the Ripper certainly adds a thematic frisson.  Letters starts each of four nights with a murder (or two).  Jack must then return from the scene of the crime to his lair by a suitably circuitous route, evading the police and laying false trails.  Meanwhile, the police try to block strategic intersections and search for clues.  It gets steadily harder for Jack as the game progresses, with fewer potential victims venturing out and fewer carriages and alleys to aid the escape.  An excellent deduction game.

Production quality is very good, while the blood spattered artwork is very evocative.  The rules are admirably clear and concise.

RATING: 1
This game sucks. Let me explain. If the player playing Jack is any level of smart the game breaks. It will be boring for Jack because the cops will never have a chance. You will end up spending an hour or two just writing a series of numbers on a paper and watching the cops wander around uselessly. They will stumble on clues but will never catch you. They are too spread out to be of any use. Far to easy to throw them off. This makes playing Jack suck. It's boring at best.

How about playing the police? Well, how about we just say boring and frustrating. You spend an hour or two taking turns saying...is there a clue at this spot? Is there a clue at this spot? Is there a clue at this spot? You run around slowly and uselessly for an hour or two and your only interaction between players is Jack mostly just saying no. No. No. No. Yes. Really? This was supposed to be fun? 

Let me ask a couple questions here. Why the heck are people rating this game WHO HAVE NOT PLAYED IT! WHY? Your rating of something you know nothing about will effect somebody's decision to buy it. Why do people give it a good rating and then in their review talk about how bad it was. DONT GIVE A GOOD REVIEW IF IT WASNT GOOD! I guess I should have read a lot more into all the comments. I guess a rating can't be trusted. If everybody played before reviewing the game and was honest in their review the rating would drop drastically. Also if people had any idea what ratings meant it would drop. You can't give it high marks on stars and give a crapy review. Lower the stars to match what you actually thought of the game. 

I got this because of the high rating and a favorable review from the Dice Tower. I don't blame the Dice Tower or Tom. He is one guy. He can love the game and I can hate it. But here I thought that nearly 10,000 ratings have to balance out and give an accurate picture of the game. I was wrong.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
At the time of my exposure to this game, I have little experience with hidden movement deduction games (Fury of Dracula, Scotland Yard, etc).

Somehow, while fun, this game seems to be somewhat too luck (although without chance) based for the detectives, due to the very slow movement and large map (not that fixing either of these will be without even worse side effects).  Even for the Ripper, it seems a little too luck dependent.

I do not always mind luck.  It has its place.  And honestly, this game is its place as well (real detectives and real criminals do have a large "chanceless" luck component to their roles).  But as a game to play for recreation, this misses for.  If it were shorter it would possibly be much better.

However, when enough luck hits right and a good chase is on, then this game has a serious AP threat -- and not a 'deep' AP, just a mechanical turn the crank AP.

Still there is good fun here and the theme for me felt well done.  I won't buy it, but I'll play it again if it comes up.  I would probably rather try Fury of Dracula (if you are going with theme and immersion, that seems even moreso) or Scotland Yard (if not, then this seems the trimmer more elegant take).

RATING: 8
This is the absolute best game in the chase/deduction genre.

Jack and the Investigators play a very different game, and playing each side is quite a different experience. Anyway, what both sides share is the edge-of-your seat tension all along the game! It's a battle of wits with constant bluffing and trying to be smarter than the opponent(s).

The only major drawback is its lenght: Whitechapel can last 3+ hours, although with all the tension it seems shorter for sure!

Another drawback lies in the "alpha player" problem, common to many coops: there is no mechanic to hinder the dominant/expert player taking over the investigation. But since I guess it will be played mostly 2p or with my alpha-player-only group, I guess this wouldn't be a very big nuisance...

RATING: 7
First play: Rules are absorbed fairly quickly and they are quite intuitive so 20 mins later we were off.

Yes its very similar to Fury of Dracula and Scotland Yard. However the Ripper has a couple of movement bonuses with the coach and alleys which make him damn near impossible to pin down. The police have to try to locate the Rippers house and so know where he is returning to each night or just plain arrest him by predicting where he is.

In our Game the Ripper won, and it seemed a real struggle for the police to close the net on him. The Ripper moves faster than the Police and the coach and alleys enable him to back track very easily. People are saying its easy to catch the Ripper, after my first game  I must disagree.

Clever and well produced but it seems more exciting to be the Ripper. Lots of banter which was alot of fun but not sure if I'd want to play again.

RATING: 7
Great game! 

The police needs to be able to comunicate and cooperate, otherwise they're in for a hard game.

As Jack, you need to be the meanest and most sneaky player at the table. Getting to your hideout is no good if you get too quickly and there are only so many places you could have gone. Misdirecting the police and making them go on blind chases around the board is extremely 

I think the best player count is 2-3 players, but 5-6 players seems doable IF all the detectives like deduction games and actually discuss their moves. As a player, I feel like I'm contributing even if I'm not actually doing something meaningful with my pawn if I'm helping the whole team by sharing insights and what I think Jack is doing. However, I can understand people getting frustrated if that happens to them, or if Jack is misdirecting them and they can't get to find clues.

RATING: 5
The game is initially fun but provides diminishing returns rather quickly (at least it did for me).  Be prepared for boredom if you play with slow or AP-prone players -- the game needs momentum otherwise it becomes rather monotonous, especially as the investigator(s).  When you are hot on Jack's trail, it can be exciting but there is too much grunt work.  Oddly, it is almost worse when a really good player is Jack.  Smart play will yield very few clues so it becomes repetitive:

"I'm looking for clues at 144"
"No"
"Looking for clues at 146"
"No"
(repeat ad nauseum)

The game can be fun (and is usually fun when playing Jack) but, by and large, there are other games I'd rather play.

RATING: 8

If you're upset that FoD is OOP and you can't get your hands on it, then pick up Letters from Whitechapel.  Personally, I think FFG has no intentions of keeping both of these games in print at the same time.  And for hidden movement fans, LETTERS is a helluva consolation prize.

RATING: 10
The three hours of playtime seemed like an hour in the first game. Although it felt tense throughout, it seems the balance tilts slightly in favor of Jack with his extra move options (carriage, lantern) giving him a slight edge. We agreed to play a variant that provides the detective player a needed option of moving detectives over hunting for clues when they seem to be fading from the hunt. I think this will balance things out nicely. Beautiful theme in this cat and mouse classic-to-be, pure deduction and bluffing game. UPDATE: In our second game, investigators win at end of long first night with Jack one move away from his hideout. Jack player chose a long route, which if a little shorter may have worked for him. We did play the variant that detectives can opt to move one more space rather than search in an obviously useless area. Seems to tweak the scales back to a very balanced game, but more plays needed to confirm this. UPDATE: After 7 more games, the last 6 plays have been with no variant and game play was quite balanced and tense for both players. It seems the investigator players(s) needs to be more experienced than or as experienced as the Jack player. Missing one opportunity for a clue can open up an escape for Jack. Contrary to the suggestion in the rule booklet, I think it's better to have the new player play Jack. When the players are even, it is a tense, close game, but the investigators cannot make mistakes. Right now, I would only allow the investigators the additional move option (variant) when that player is inexperienced.

RATING: 10
Sept 10, 2011: The art and theme are extremely evocative. It's a game dripping with theme. It's a bit creepy to play Jack's character as his goals are obviously heinous.

What makes this game shine compared to other cat-and-mouse games (e.g. Scotland Yard) is that it is equally fun to play both roles.

There is a lot of tension playing this game. Though it can take 3 hours to play, the time goes by quickly because the game is so engaging.

Feb 17, 2012 update: 8.5 -> 9.0. After 6 plays, the gameplay is holding up and the games are memorable. We talk about the game afterwards and there is a story to each game. This seems to be the best cat-n-mouse game out there.

We play with 2 variants: detectives get the optional rush movement, and Jack can't kill the same wretched more than once.

Update: we've added a 3rd variant: we start at Roman V and Jack is not allowed home until 1. But after playing a few more games, it feels too stacked against Jack.

Update Sept 2012: 9->9.5 After 16 plays, the game is stilling invigorating. The final "balance" is: include the rush variant, Jack can't kill the same wretched more than once, and on the fourth night, he can't be home on the first move.

Update June 2017: revisited after a few years. Provisionally bumped it to a 10. We can't think of another boardgame that is as mano-a-mano as LfW. It is tense and intense the whole time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Letters from Whitechapel reminded me of Scotland Yard, but it took around 2 hours more… Not cool! 3+ hours is too much for this game, but I still enjoyed myself and would like to try it again. Still, I have a few concerns:

Letters from Whitechapel seems to favor Jack by quite a bit. I’m not saying Jack is going to win every time, not at all, but I’m confident that Jack has the upper hand throughout this game. My only play was with 3 new players against an experienced Jack, and we searched a lot of places except for the right ones in the two first nights… And this is one of my main criticisms; there are just too many options/paths Jack can choose, and it comes down to chance which way the investigators think Jack went; yes, I know, they can try to deduce, and most certainly it will help them, but there are just so many times Jack can move either to spot A, B or C, so you need to take a chance, and you might get lucky or not… This isn’t something I fancy in such a long game! The actual deduction is also limited because of the sheer amount of places Jack can be. And if you think you have a clue about where he is, he can just use a double move or diagonal move and be gone quite easily… We did play with some minor house-rules which make it a little easier for the detectives, but I understand that they are universally accepted, and I wouldn’t ever play without them as the game favors Jack from the beginning. 

So, all in all, because of the length and because the game is heavily skewed in Jack’s favor (this is not just my opinion but seems to be the consensus here on BGG), I cannot rate it more than a 5… I am going to try to get it to the table in August to verify my first-hand impressions, and if I still feel the same, I am probably going to lower my rating by one.

RATING: 10
This game is awesome. It is everything Fury of Dracula should have been. Trim all the fat from Fury of Dracula and distill it down into just the chase/hunt and you could very well have this game.  The rules are simple and could be played quite easily with people 'outside' the hobby.  The tension and enjoyment-per-time is exceptionally high. I can't see anybody who even has a slight interest in deduction games not loving this one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I played Scotland Yard when I was a kid and really liked it. Letters from Whitechapel strikes me as Scotland Yard for grownups. But that's not to say it's a simple re-theming. Instead, it takes the basic principle of Scotland Yard and adds some very clever mechanics that add tension and theme. Jack and the cops move on separate but overlapping grids. And, Jack gets to play the waiting game each night before he chooses a victim.

It's a shame this game is out of print, because I really have enjoyed my few plays. Most of those have been as a two-player game, and it works surprisingly well with one player as Jack and the other player controlling all the cops. It also streamlines game play because there's no need for the cops to table talk. One play with four players certainly added to the length, but it was also fun to see the cops work it out.

Either way, with two players or more, I can say that this game produces a surprising amount of stress and tension for Jack, which I consider fantastic. I certainly wouldn't pay $80 or $100 for this game, but if you can snag a copy, do it.

RATING: 5
2 plays 2 player

After a single night learning game and a two complete games as the police, I like the game but remain hesitant.  It is a fine hide-and-seek style game, good theme fit, but perhaps a bit overwrought with the token related portions of the game.

Right now, it seems very hard for the police to succeed but I look forward to trying again.  The detectives are slow, and it proves difficult to find clues or seal off areas before Jack has an opportunity to get back to his lair.  We will probably only play again with optional rules.

Our 2 player games took about 75 minutes.  I can not imagine playing the game with 4+ players.

We will play again, but the game did not leave me with that "one more game" immediate urge.

RATING: 8
So far I've played one game as Jack and it was an incredibly intense game from my perspective (arrested on the final turn). I found it an extremely challenging game as a single player against the collective brains of five other gamers. I do think however that the games designers did need to stipulate some time-limits on analysis for the police. Ultimately I think it is a solvable puzzle (more or less so depending on how many clues the investigators find) given enough time to work on it. We allowed the investiogators a couple of minutes after Jack returned home to try work out what had happened. But I think it is certainly a game you can improve upon with experience on both sides of the game. I know I would certainly choose my base differently in a second game and I (and the investigators) learnt a lot about movement subtlties during the game. It was quite interesting showing them where I had actually gone each night after the game. So it's a game well worth repeat plays, just decide before the game how long you want to allow investigators to discuss and analyze stuff during the game. Also I would highly reccomend printing out a better map for Jack to reference for his movement during the game. Look at the board is not an option and the map provided is a bit on the small side. Based on 1 play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
One of the greatest unsolved mysteries in the history of heinous crimes is the horrific series of gruesome murders committed by the infamous Jack the Ripper. This calculating villain wielded a knife with the precision of a scalpel as he stained the streets with the blood of the “Wretched”… London’s ladies of the night.

This board game brings the historical event to life with alarming historical accuracy. It captures everything from the names and visages of the police to the harrowing letters that Jack wrote taunting his would be captors. One player controls Jack, moving in secret, while the other players command the forces of the law.

The game only touched the table once, but in a single play it earned James’ respect as the most fun he’d had playing a board game in recent memory. The game is riveting because the stakes are so high and at any moment the police could catch Jack. Just as easily however, Jack can slip away forever.

RATING: 8
[2012-02-26 Rating: n.a. -> 8]
For quite some time I was looking for a game with a play style similar to Scotland Yard. I did try Fury of Dracula but with all the cards, special abilities and fights, this was just a little too much for me. When I finally got Letters from Whitechapel (LfW) to the table, I kind of knew that the search was over. Ruleswise, this is a very easy game (right now, I'm able to teach it in about five to ten minutes) that has a all the deduction fun of Scotland Yard.  Although I have not played LfW as Jack yet, it is a keeper for me. I enjoyed every session of it and I find it always fascinating with how little information the police can deduce Jack's hideout and how the tension rises if you can or can't find a clue. Great!

If I had to name something that could turn people off, then it would be a) the play time and b) the fact that you kind of need the right group. 

Let's start with b) first: This game is basically a two player game with one guy controlling the police and chasing the serial killer played by another guy. In order to make it work with more than two players, you do need people that are really paying attention and involved in the game all the time, discussing about certain moves and everything else. Otherwise it is very easy for someone playing on the police side to "go with flow" and do whatever someone tells them to do. I guess, this player won't enjoy the game very much. So I do always try to keep everyone on the police side involved, which isn't too difficult in my gaming group.

Since people playing on the police side should be really discussing their moves, the play time can really go up. We had games that lasted 3.5 hours. Which would be my possible complaint a). But since there's a lot of tension, I don't mind a long play time here.

To sum it up: If you do like deduction, give LfW a try.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This may be the best game I have ever played. Massively exciting every time.

Edit: Dropped to a 9. As our understanding of the game deepened and our skills as both Jack and the constables increased, the game has gotten really, really long. 

It's still a fantastic game, I just wish it was shorter - or had a short game variant - so it would get to the table more often. But I f you like long games and deduction I couldn't recommend this more highly.

Edit: dropped to an 8. I love asymmetrical games but with more plays the experience of playing each side has diverged too much. The constables work their butts off and still have to rely on getting a couple lucky breaks. Playing Jack is infinitely easier and, once you've picked up a few key moves, almost impossible to lose. And as the Jack player you really need to bring a book to deal with downtime.

I still can't help loving the game but I really hope the upcoming expansion solves some of the problems.

EDIT: This is now the most precipitous ratings drop I've ever made. I'm convinced the game is broken. If Jack plays to win, he can't lose. The game depends entirely on his willingness to taunt the police. If he does not taunt them, he cannot be found.

RATING: 9
Very enjoyable hunt/evade deduction game. A simple streamlined rule set and excellent components.

I like.

Update - 3 games now and I haven't seen a 'Jack' win yet - but still lots of fun. If it proves to have longevity I will raise to a 9 - a bit worried it might reveal all of it's charms and secrets in just a handful of plays. Update - 9 games of this now and still enjoying it (still haven't seen a 'Jack' win). Rating up to a 9.

For me this is at it's best as a 3-player game - 2 detectives v 1 Jack. The game plays fine with 2, but loses some of the 'fun' when there is no teamwork element and table talk amongst detective players. 4-player, i.e. 3 detectives, might work as long as none of the detectives is too dominant - I would avoid games with 4-5 detectives, some of the detectives will likely be mariginalised.

when I've played this the detectives have always played as a coop team - i.e. there has been agreement on moving all of the pawns - no one has just controlled their own detectives.

I really don't understand the claims of this is too easy for Jack - this is a deduction game, not really a chase game, so get deducting - not finding a clue can tell you as much sometimes as finding a clue, I think some groups are not thinking enough.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
One player plays Jack The Ripper, who is attempting to murder the "wretched" and escape home on each of four nights.  The other players play cooperatively as the detectives, trying to prevent Jack from getting home, or even arrest him.  Simple game based on Scotland Yard, where Jack is recording his moves on a notepad, while the detectives and potential victims move across the game board.  Nice ideas, including that Jack and the victims move on a different trail to the detectives.  The mechanics are simple, meaning the players can concentrate on the strategy.  Jack is basically heading home - either via a direct route or using something more circuitous to throw the detectives off.  The detectives are covering swathes of the board, hoping to pick up a clue regarding Jack's movements, or indeed eliminating sections of the board as Jack's potential hideout purely from the absence of any sign of Jack.  The game ends when Jack survives four nights, or the detectives bottle him up before that.  The presentation of the game is rather sanguine, but gorgeous - you could hang the board as art.  A lot to like here.  A superb game pitched between Scotland Yard and Fury of Dracula.

RATING: 7
This is probably the best game in the no-frills hidden movement and deduction genre, mechanistically speaking.  I think I would have preferred a lighter or more fantastical theme though.  This may be hypocritical given some other games that I enjoy.  However, I don't like importing "colonists" to farm tobacco in Puerto Rico, and I don't relish murdering women in this one.  Go figure.

That all being said, I love the central conceit that Jack needs to return to a single hideout space each turn.  This gives a wonderful centerpiece for deduction and deceit, and the maps feels very much the map up on the wall of every crime drama.  This game is wonderfully tense and just all around solid and elegant. Perhaps appropriately, this game has murdered Scotland Yard and Nuns on the Run.  Fury of Dracula survives thanks to its delicious chrome, and I suspect a Specter Ops will as well if I ever get a chance to play it. 

UPDATE Sep 2015
I am getting an inkling that Jack can win through a series of fairly cheap moves.  My last game, The  investigators found only a few clues the whole game, and  don' know that I would have done nay better in their shoes.  I pulled a little double back the first nigh, then with that false trail in place was able to get to my hideout fairly quickly on night 3, and in 3 moves (2 wih a carriage) on night 4.  When Jack waits, the women must move, and that can let you reuse a murder spot to your convenience.  So maybe the game can be too easy for Jack, but I won't get to play enough to know, because my wife finds the game frustrating, and I can understand that.

I don't like that the game can be made easier or harder for Jack just by where he chooses to put his hideout.  It makes the app almost a necessity, but then they want another $3 for that.  Not a lot of money if you love the game, but I would wish it was somehow better balanced here. Ultimately this is the point that will probably push the game out of my collection.  Jack needs to consider how hard they want to make the game for themselves at the very start, and that will result in second-guessing whether that was a good choice, win or lose.

If I'm feeling in the mood to outwit somebody than I will snag this game off the shelf. This is a gorgeous game involving Jack the Ripper so, I enjoy the "realism", or at least the real-life reference. One player controls Jack (via hidden movement) as all the others try and determine, through deduction, which way he went - did he backtrack, did he move right past us, where is he!?! Great tension and can be nerve racking for the Jack player.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I’ve never been a fan of cooperative games, but the one-against-many aspect here adds something that makes it a much more engaging experience.  I found the players much more engaged in this game and emotionally more involved in the decision making than any other cooperative game (the discussions amongst the police/detectives became quite heated!).  In most co-op games, many just sit back and let the alpha-player make all the best moves to defeat the game.  However, here it felt much more real and engaging when Jack announced his move rather than just turning over a randomly shuffled deck of cards to see what plague-cubes were spread to what continents on a map.  

The idea of randomly selecting a lead-detective is also a great away to avoid the alpha player problem and also shows itself as a necessity as here players are much more engaged in this game than I’ve ever seen in any co-op game before.  Here every one seems to care a lot more.  

The only problem I really found was the game’s length.  The box says 120 minutes, but we played this close to 3 ½ hours.  The issue was that the detectives were so emotionally invested in the game that AP started to settle in.  Next time, I would set up a five minute timer for the detectives (especially when played at higher player counts).  It’s no fun for Jack to sit there while the detectives squabble for 15 minutes especially when you have some players drawing out decision-trees and doing probability analysis.  

Oh, and then there is the Rulebook: the mechanics of the game are very simple and streamlined, why can’t the rulebook reflect that?  In its current form, it reads more like a 1st edition Dungeon Master’s Guide!

RATING: 6
Very thematic game with asymmetric play. I'm not a big fan of 1-vs-many games but this is a good implementation with a lot of tension. 

Couple of things to consider... To me, there's much more tension playing as Jack and given the choice, I would always play as him. Playing as the Police is a shared goal, open information cooperative game, which are not bad, but not my favorite and I would actively avoid playing as the Police, with more than one other player. If the Police aren't able to hone in on Jack's hideout after the first 2 rounds, it can degenerate into guessing and can also last up to 3 hours, which is a bit too long for this type of game.

Quite a bit of constructive feedback, but overall, this is a solid game and I do enjoy playing it as Jack, or with a small team of Police.

RATING: 8
This is the best deduction game I've played so far, hands down. I've been holding out hope for getting Fury of Dracula back out one day, but with this one around, I'm not sure there's reason to. The system for checking clues is clever and creates some good tension and decisions for how the detectives go about picking up the trail. And I like that you don't necessarily have to catch Jack to realize some type of success. Each night gives you new information so you have an idea where he'll be going back to. This tightens the net and heightens the tension.

Have to relate my once-in-a-lifetime session: I won the game on the second turn. Yes, turn 2. I correctly deduced which route my clever friend would take on his first move and found a clue confirming such. Then I correctly deduced that since I was on to his trail, he would move back to the original crime scene so as not to leave another clue marker. It would be the clever move; he's a clever guy. I arrested the crime scene spot and game over, baby. Oh, that can be so satisfying to read one of your friends like that.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I love, love, love:
- the purity of the hidden movement mechanism
- only occasional twists in the game (carriages and alleyways)
- the theme (anything about the Ripper has always been fascinating)
- the cooperative deduction puzzle, like in the style of detectives
- the sweat of almost being caught or not knowing where Jack is
- the agony of choosing between asking for a clue or arresting
- that the detectives move up to two squares and Jack moves (usually) one circle, so it's not like it's very difficult to catch up to him
- that there is added stuff to change up the game, like the letters, but we still haven't tried them...

I dislike:
- feeling like there's little chance I'm going to win when playing as a detective
- those carriages/alleyways making me feel like there's NO chance of finding Jack
- that the ladies are called "The Wretched"--whyyyy?

Final verdict: Great game that uses the hidden movement mechanism simply but very well with solid theme and plenty of tension with the game always ending in lots of talking about what just happened.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Based on about 30 plays, I am giving this a 9.5.  I have only two other games rated 9 or higher in my collection.  I can't stop thinking about this game.  I wrote a full review but here are the basics:
Wonderfully integrated and executed theme. Finally found something to push Agricola and Le Havre off the table.  Top-notch components.  Fairly well-balanced; Jack has won a little more than half our games, without the optional rules that give him additional help. I disagree with those who say it is imbalanced, although with two players, Jack does seem to win more often. With multiple detectives, it is much tougher for Jack. Replayability factor seems high.  Two negatives: it's a bit long, and a few rules are not clearly articulated in the book.

Edited 11/28/11.

RATING: 6

They get fewer, less helpful clues, often move slower than Jack, and generally seem to stand little chance of apprehending the murderer unless they get lucky or manage to piece together a sufficiently clear picture of Jack's hideout over the course of several nights.

So there is the reason why you can't just play one or two nights right there which unfortunately means that the game runs a little longer than it should. Especially if the first nights work poorly for the detectives the remaining murders can drag out much longer than needed.

As is often the case in 1-vs-many games, playing the one, Jack, is the more interesting proposition than the detectives. Playing with more than three players (which would leave at least one player in control of a single detective only) is therefore not advisable. With just two players, I'd rate this at least a full point higher.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I'm a big Sherlock Holmes fan, and enjoy pretty much anything set in Victorian London.  The theme in this game is so well integrated that I truly feel like I'm on the hunt for Jack the Ripper... or, as is quite often the case, that I *am* Jack.  :P  Every detail of this game is perfect and the quality is excellent.  My only complaint is that my wife doesn't like the game as much as I do.  :)

I've never played a game like this before where I've felt real tension.  Playing as Jack with a smart group of detectives is nerve wracking and exciting.  I once circled around and stood immediately behind a police officer hoping they wouldn't turn around and reinspect the area.  When they turned right and walked down an alley I had to stifle a sigh of relief as I stepped into my hideout and survived another night.

Fantastic game, fantastic mechanics, fantastic theme.  I can't wait to play this game again.

UPDATE:  11 games in and Jack has never won.  We've added the letters to the game from now on and the detectives are not allowed to rush in.  I still love the game, from both sides.  I find it to be a very tense and pure battle of wits.  I've stopped playing this beyond 2p.  It's absolutely PERFECT as a 2p game.

UPDATE: Specter Ops is a similar game with a more family-friendly theme, with more variety in hunter and agent powers, that plays in less time.  LoW is an excellent 2p pure deduction battle of wits, but it tends to drag out from AP.  Specter Ops may replace this, or it may sit alongside this.  We shall see.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Pure suspense.
If you're the person playing the part of Jack The Ripper, you're in for a nail-biting night of close calls as you listen in while your friends discuss their strategies, search for clues to your whereabouts, and tighten the ever collapsing noose around you and your murderous escapades in the foggy London night. You'll be inwardly terrified, but outwardly calm as someone makes a random arrest in the very spot were you were standing only moments before. You'll try on your best poker-faced mask of deception as they question you, suddenly realizing that you've been too careless in picking your targets- too obvious in your choice of escape routes. You may have escaped tonight, but tomorrow, there's more "work" to be done. And they're getting closer to finding your hideout. It's only a matter of time...
If you're playing as one of the investigators, you'll smugly ask the player representing Jack if he fled down a particular alley leading to a squat neighborhood of houses- one of which is his home. You're sure he did, you know you've got him this time, but when he truthfully replies, "Nope, nevah been there, Gov,nah!" your expression curdles. 
You blink at the board as a whole crucial night of police work collapses. You've lost him. 
"No," you whisper, "that's impossible."

But it isn't. Jack is smarter than you thought, because in this game he's being controlled by a real person. A real CUNNING person, who backtracked to the scene of the crime and led you on a merry chase- ducking down dark alleys, and passing within inches of your handlebar mustache. He's slipped through your fingers once again, and tomorrow night another wretched streetwalker will pay for YOUR mistake.

This is an awesome game. You'll play it once as Jack and be instantly hooked on the adrenaline buzz it provides. After that, you'll want to try it as an investigator, because you think you've learned all the tricks.
But you're wrong. You don't know all the tricks, because there's no hunt quite like stalking a real human opponent, and each person will play it different: smart, risky, clever, misleading. 
And sometimes, they just get lucky.

The mark of a good game is how well it creates a simulation of an experience that real life, and/or polite society simply does not permit, and Whitechapel is almost too effective at letting you into the mind of a serial killer. Don't believe me? Let me give you a little insight into the last time I played as Jack:

"There's nothing quite like the thrill of planing my next attack, being hunted, and then dodging their every attempt to contain me. My heart beats in my chest. I have all this nervous energy that I don't know what to do with. It's such a rush! But I have to play it cool when they question me about my movements, because any hint of anxiety, any subtle "tell" that I demonstrate in my body language- they'll pick up on it. Of course, I can use this to my advantage, and misdirect them using the exact same methods. In a way, I begin to feel sorry for them as they fumble about in confusion. I start secretly wishing they would catch me. It's exhausting work keeping up this level of deception. Besides, if they don't catch me, another night will come, and deep down, I know.
I know I'm going to kill again..."

Play this game at night, with candles, and someone who can really act the part of Jack. It will leave your skin crawling, and you''ll pound the table with genuine rage when he gives you the slip. Just make sure you don't get too caught up in the moment. Did the passage above creep you out?
How do you think I feel? I experienced it first hand, and it made me very self-conscious of my actions and behaviors for the rest of the night.
I'm not kidding. 
Be careful my children. When you play on the blood-soaked streets of Whitechapel, someone's very likely to get hurt.

RATING: 9
Letters from Whitechapel has a philosophy I was searching for a long time in a game. First of all the board is awesome. It's detailed, with really nice artwork, and also big, making wanna try the game anyways. The way it uses deduction and bluffing is so much clever. The secret movement mechanic is unique giving the game an excellent experience for all the players.

 I have played games both being Jack and the police and you cant imagine how much intense the game was for me both times. Being Jack is maybe the best part. You are listening to the police, you see how they think, you try to confuse them and escape. You are invisible to them, making it easier to take risks and have fun with them like an authentic killer :P.. Being the police also needs concentration and well organizing your movements. You are chasing an invisible killer having nothing but some clues where he had previously been. You must be fast, unpredictable and patient.

I can find a disadvantage in this game (although easily overlapped). First of all it works best only with 2 players and not more. If more people are playing the police, it  is sometimes difficult to take a common decision especially when there is one player who knows the game well (or thinks he knows it) making boring and not fun at all for the rest of the police. Although, if your playing group can find middle ground solutions, so that everyone is happy with the  strategy of the police, and no one tries to establish his opinion and only, this disadvantage is overlapped. Last, I think that if the player who plays Jack is careful, he will always win. Most times though he wont. As I said being Jack makes you a real killer wanting to take risks and confuse the police, and not searching always the easy way to win. But even if this is happening with your group, you can just use the optional rules to help the police to make the game balanced!

In conclusion, I highly recommend this game especially for Jack the ripper and deduction game lovers. It's a unique experience and I'm always looking forward for another game. Have fun!!!

RATING: 10
I love detective shows and novels, especially stories from 19th century England. So the theme is perfect for me. Really though, the game wins for the thrill of the chase. The first time we played everyone commented about the adrenaline rush you get when the detectives are closing in on you, or vice versa when you know you have the ripper cornered to two or three circles but you don't know exactly which one. You have to have a good strategy as the detectives, but once you figure that out you will always have a few chances to catch him each time.

Update: After 5 plays this is downgraded a bit from 10 to 8. A good Jack is awfully hard to catch which has frustrated a member of my gaming group (of the three of us, he is the only Jack who has been caught). I still think it is a great game. Look forward to seeing how it plays after 5 more rounds. 

Update 2: After 8 plays and 3 house rules this is back to a 10. This game, more than any I have played needs adjustments at first based on the skill of Jack. We have 1 guy who is not that good as Jack and we play the standard rules. 2 of us are fairly cunning and we have implemented a requirement to stack clue tokens if you visit a location multiple times. I used to circle around in similar spots and then break out which was very effective. We also require at least 2 moves from a murder scene before getting home and we have randomized the hideout. With these adjustments I was able to catch the other cunning Jack. We will scale back our house rules once the police winning pct gets to at least 50. The last few plays with these rules have been marvelous and I believe this may be my favorite game at the moment.

Update 3: I am 20 plays in. The other day I caught my buddy 2 out of 3 times using the standard rule set. Playing as the cops successfully is very daunting at first, however over time you develop strategies. As you play more with Jack you begin to anticipate the best moves that Jack may make in a given situation. This game definitely gets better with age, absolutely love it.

RATING: 8
(08/10/2014) Comment based on three consecutive plays.

This is a simple and elegant, GREAT manhunt game, with plenty of cat-and-mouse, skin-of-your-teeth tracking and deduction, brain-melty tension. However, there are strong suspicions that this will not scale well beyond two or three players.

I've played Scotland Yard, Fury of Dracula, Mystery at the Abbey, and Code Name Sector, all earlier manhunt/tracking/mystery games. Code Name Sector is the earliest, where a computer controlled the movement of the submarine--nice, but became quite mechanical after a few plays. Mystery at the Abbey was boring, as it was more of a deduction game closer to Clue than a manhunt tracking game. Fury of Dracula had a good hidden movement implementation, but with attacks and broad playing area, had too much downtime, and very little meaningful play that it was equally boring. Scotland Yard was a good implementation of hidden movement, but lacked the tracking aspect and was too simplistic. So, normally I stay away from deduction, cooperative, and mystery games.

Compared to those earlier games, Letters from Whitechapel is the best manhunt game in a board game implementation (although one of the players mentioned that LfW is comparable to Garibaldi: The Escape--same designer). Play time can vary, depending on the AP of the players, and can be easily compounded with more players, like any cooperative. Playing Jack the Ripper, also, can be an AP nightmare. It is literally a race for five detectives to find clues and try to capture Jack the Ripper before he escapes to the safety of his hideout.

The movement mechanics are simple and intuitive, as well as innovative. The board is the street map of Whitechapel, with 195 numbered circles scattered throughout the board, each connected by dotted lines that represent movement along the streets. Between the circles lie one, two, or three consecutive black squares, usually at intersections or right angles. This means that there are essentially two different movement maps, one with circles where JtR and the "wretched" women wander around, and the one with squares where the detectives travel on. The detectives have the advantage in numbers, but poor mobility as they are constrained to move along the black squares. JtR can move from circle to circle, enjoying comparatively better mobility, especially at intersections. However, he can be slowed to a crawl in neighborhoods off the main road, allowing the detectives to catch up. To balance this, he also has a limited number of one-time use bonus tokens that he can use to achieve faster movement (riding the coach) or flexibility (traveling through alleys).

The game is played in four rounds, where each round JtR kills one (two in the third round) of the "wretched" women that start from fixed positions on the board. From there, JtR secretly plots what number circle(s) he is moving to. Then, the five detectives (each or several manipulated by a player for 1 to 5 players) each openly move on the board along the black squares. They call out the numbered circles they are adjacent to, and if JtR had ever passed through a given numbered circle, they can mark that spot as a "clue". After finding a clue, it becomes an intense game of hide-and-seek, as the detectives try to maneuver to encircle JtR, and JtR tries to outfox and slip out of the closing noose and escape. It is fascinating to watch the chase unfold on the board, as clue markers start to populate the board and show the general whereabouts of the killer.

And just as quickly, JtR can slip by a detective by riding a coach, or lose a tracking detective by moving through alleys to show up unexpectedly on the other side of a given city block and lose his pursuers. However, each round, JtR has fewer and fewer bonus tokens, and the starting locations of his potential victims becomes less and less, thus making it progressively riskier and more difficult for JtR. Fortunately for JtR, the detectives are generally forced to start each round at their present locations, but they have some ability to relocate up to two detectives to default starting locations.

The tension as the JtR player is very high, where he must anticipate where the detectives will move and search, and make his moves accordingly. He has the bonus tokens to shake off his pursuers, but has precious few of them and must use them sparingly and judiciously. The detectives can cooperate and coordinate, but their limited movement can be frustrating. They must also be very careful in searching for clues, as the calling out of numbered circles will end for a given detective, as soon as a clue is discovered, whether it is fresh or several turns old. The tension for the detectives grows equally high as they close-in on JtR, but with higher risk that JtR will slip by/outflank one or several of them. If JtR slips through the cracks, usually there is nothing that the detectives can do to stop him from retreating to his hideout. They will have a clue as to the general neighborhood(s) where JtR's hideout is, and use that in planning for the next round. Of course, if all the clues point to JtR being adjacent to a detective, the detective can attempt to make an arrest. If JtR is indeed at the indicated circle, the game is over and the detectives win. If not, that detective's turn is over.

This game was so enjoyable that with three players, we played the game three times consecutively, with each of us playing JtR. There are no random elements or game mechanisms that get in the way. This is purely a chess match and poker game rolled into one, with mind against mind(s). The euphoria in capturing JtR is a great gamer's high. The euphoria in eluding capture as JtR is even a greater high. Although playing a full four-turn game clocked in at about 2.5 hours, the payoff is immense. Both hunter and the hunted will find an extremely pleasurable gaming experience, if they are into tracking and evasion. It taps the primordial Id and ego, as both sides brazenly think they can outwit the other. As Jack, it is easy to shift into megalomania, taunting and tempting the detectives; he secretly wants to be caught, but feels sorry as he watches the bumbling detectives flail blindly. He wants a challenge, so he may take undue risks and go right up to a detective, get "detected" and then get an adrenaline rush by quickly evading, while simultaneously stroking his ego by confirming his intellectual superiority. As far as theme, this game drips with it, but we can easily see a number of equally exciting re-themes: sub versus destroyer(s)--think Enemy Below or Balance of Terror in Star Trek:TOS, classic fox and hound scenarios, or the movie Alien--think crew members try to track an alien on board a space ship, but the alien may kill you.

The "Alpha Male" syndrome that plagues most cooperative games is somewhat addressed with one of the detectives randomly designated "The Boss" each round. The sweet spot may be two or three players at most--the two non-JtR players can control two or three detectives (we played two apiece and shared one between us, as well as rotating the "boss" title, instead of the random draw) each for more flexibility and fun. If this gets to six players, it will be one detective per player, and the range of options will be far less interesting--there is risk that one of the detectives (every round) will be so far out of position, as to not being able to contribute meaningfully. We will have to play at larger numbers and see, but I suspect controlling only one detective may become boring very quickly--the game looks like it will not scale well with more players. This is a solid "8", with room to move up, but for only 2 or 3 players so far. With 5-6 players, players may suffer the same issues that plague other cooperatives: little meaningful play, alpha male, and long play times.

RATING: 8
規則:
*偵探永遠使用五個(小於五人則有人多控)，拿對應參考表，並洗勻偵探領袖標記疊起，拿走所有偵探棋子跟受害者棋子
*傑克拿移動記錄紙跟屏幕，放一個棋子在夜晚記錄軌第一格，並決定他的藏身處並拿取所有對應標記跟參考表
*遊戲共經過四夜，每夜分成兩個階段，只要傑克都成功脫逃回藏身處，傑克獲勝，反之偵探獲勝
**階段一 地獄**
1.傑克拿取對應此夜的特殊移動標記(3C2A/2C2A/2C1A/1C1A)
2.傑克拿取對應此夜的女人標記(All/7-4m/6-3m/4-1m)並蓋著放在圖板上紅色數字區(必須沒有犯罪標記)，有標記m的代表可能的目標
3.偵探打開領袖標記決定此回合的領袖，領袖蓋著放置七個警探標記在版圖上的黃邊黑點上，有顏色的對應之後的偵探，第二夜開始，上回合偵探在的五個位置一定要放(顏色可不一樣)，剩下放在黃點上
(傑克在此時可以使用信件功能)
4.傑克翻開所有女人標記並換上受害者棋子，時間標記放在下面羅馬數字1位置上
5.傑克選擇殺一個目標則把一個女人棋子換成犯罪標記進入8.或是 等待(時間標記到5時就不能再等)並進入6.
6. 時間標記左移一格，偵探領袖移動所有女子棋子一格(不能重疊)且不能停在跟偵探標記相鄰位置或跨過偵探標記，或是停留在有犯罪標記處
7.傑克可翻開一個偵探標記，如果是假的移除，然後回到步驟5.
8.傑克選完人殺後，標記在自己紙上，對應夜跟時間標記位置上標示數字，然後把第二個傑克棋子放在時間軌上(第三夜兩個數字分別填兩格，靠右格會起始地點)
9.偵探打改所有偵探標記並置換對應偵探棋子，也移除剩下的受害棋子
**階段二 追捕**
1.傑克移動一格數字並記錄在紙上同行右邊的空格，不能跨過偵探棋子，並移動圖板上的時間標記右邊一格，不能動直接算輸，如果移動到藏身處直接宣告並結束此階段進入下一夜，行動時間到底也直接輸，可是用特殊移動標記，使用後放在對應的時間標記上，回藏身處的移動不能用特殊移動
> 馬車 一次花兩個時間移動兩格，且可以跨過偵探，且一定要移動，不能回溯
> 小巷 可跨過城市到相鄰城區的另一個數字格
2.由偵探領袖開始輪流移動自己的偵探0~2格(黑點上)，可跨過偵探但不能停在同格
3.每個偵探由領袖開始順時鐘對周圍數字格動作二選一，階段沒結束就回1.
>找尋線索 可以對周圍數字一直問直到出現一個線索停止或全部問完，如果傑克該夜有經過過就放一個線索(特殊規則 當放上5個線索時，傑克可以拿一個假線索標記)
>執行逮補 宣告一個相鄰的數字，如果傑克在上面就獲勝，不然就沒事
*如果傑克逃脫，階段二結束，所有偵探跟犯罪標記留在場上，線索移除，傑克棋子移動到下一晚，並回到階段一
**** 額外規則 幫助傑克
*傑克的信 第二夜開始，每夜可是用一封傑克信來改變偵探標記位置
*錯誤線索 在第二階段3.時傑克可放置在任何一格數字格上，該格不能被找尋線索
**** 額外規則 幫助偵探
*奔跑 多一個行動為可幫其他尚未移動過的偵探棋子移動
*範圍逮補 改為直接逮捕周遭的所有數字格
*快來抓我 傑克不能選擇紅色數字跟相鄰格為藏身地
*我知道你的位置 如果決定偵探領袖時為棕色或紅色，可馬上執行額外逮捕行動，宣告任一個數字格，如果是傑克藏身處直接獲勝，一場遊戲只能用一次

RATING: 8
Hide n seek on a table. Great game, I like it better than Fury of Dracula, since it`s much simpler and shorter game, but it`s got that tension what makes Fury of Dracula a good game.

Important! I don`t care what people are saying, but this game is best NOT with 2, but with 6! RATING: 2
--- July 9, 2011 ---

Ugh. Deduction game. Bleh.

--- May 5, 2012 ---

I have now played a second game of Whitechapel. Honestly, I didn't expect to ever play it again, but I had a group over who were keen to give it a go. I enjoy playing pretty much any game with good company, so I was happy to give it another shot. I can't say I like the game still, but a better second experience bumps the rating from a 2 to a 4.

My rating of 4 assumes I'm playing as an investigator with at least a few other players. I like being a part of the investigation and watching the story unfold. I don't like being the sole person who has to be able to figure out and picture where everything and everybody has been, including a phantom Jack that I can't even see. The aforementioned story really is fascinating, though. The mechanics and flavor work _very_ nicely in tandem.

In all honesty, I feel bad giving the game even as low as a 4. The game itself is very well made. The components are beautiful, and the gameplay is tight. It's just not my style of game. In the end, I really feel that I should rate games based on how I feel about playing them rather than how much I respect the design.

RATING: 10
This game is incredibly fun. Simple, tense, strategic, lots of player interaction. Just a great implementation of a deduction game. It has a wonderfully designed board, well-written rulebook, and elegant components. The theme just drips right out of the box. 

Games can last a while, but they never drag due to there never being any downtime for either the investigators or Jack. I love how the two sides offer completely different experiences. The investigator side plays like a wonderful co-op mystery game, rewarding deduction and reasoning, but also encouraging strategic area control tactics, while Jack plays like a deliriously tense game of hide and seek: requiring good bluffing skills, creativity, and being cool under pressure. An awesome experience on both ends. 

I personally love this game, but it has been hard to get other people to come back to it. The game is quite long, and strategy a bit difficult to comprehend at times. I still consider this a "will always play" game for myself, so despite these misgivings, I still rate it a 10.

Initial Rating: 7.5 (November 2011)

This is an ok thematic game, still I feel it is too long for what it offers. Perhaps this is played best with 2 players, I have played with 5 players both times and the downtime is considerable (and unacceptable). I enjoy playing this at the beginning, however I feel the game get samey very quickly and it begins to drag so I’m downgrading it to a more fitting rating. If only the game didn’t ran for 4 nights :(. I feel that Jack can have more fun that the investigators.

The game is fairly simple to teach, but the play time exceeds the 3 hour mark and that is a big negative for me, also the game can be broken easily if the Jack player makes a mistake.

In my experience, the Jack player has a hard time avoiding the investigators, so I would advocate to always play with the optional cards.

The component´s quality is pretty good.

Bottom line, a decent game, one that I have no desire to own or play too often, perhaps once a year is ok for me.

RATING: 7.5

______
Nicely produced asymmetrical catch the hidden escapee game in the same genre as Garibaldi and (the Daddy) Scotland Yard.

In effect there are 4 escapes (rather than the single escape in Garibaldi/SY) which adds interest and variety, as does Jack choosing a different hideout (the space to which he escapes) each game. 

Jack must think ahead to which of his victims he wants to take in which location on each of the escapes, and try to lure the detectives away from his hideout as the detectives carry most of their positions forward from the end of one escape to the start of the next. (Identifying the rough location of Jack's hideout and clustering 'tecs around it seems an obvious strategy for the detectives).

Get past the dubious theme and it's a good game, Like most of these games I suspect it's at its best in pure game terms as a 2 player game, but it's good social fun as multiplayer game. Again like most games with a co-operative element the detectives have to work in unison which can be a challenge. I suspect like most co-op games it could suffer if one detective tries to take over the decision making (the game rotates the lead detective from escape to escape to try to avoid this), which would turn it into a 2-player game with some frustrated observers. (This hasn't happened in my plays to date but I could see it if there is a domineering player in the detective team).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.8
This is a cleaver game. One player is hidden from the other players and all the information you start with is where he starts (killed). the hidden player (jack the ripper) must then make it back to his hideout. a place chosen by him at the begging of the game. he must do this three times (3 rounds) to win the game. everyone else is detectives on the board trying to capture jack. this is done by trying to find where jack has been as apposed to actually nailing down his exact position. the first two round are spent trying to workout where jacks hideout is and the third is spent trying to prevent him from reaching it and if possible capturing him.
jack and the detectives use different dots on the board to move so the detectives can never be on the same space as jack (the spaces next to the detectives can be investigated).
i love this game!! in one game the detectives managed to catch my rout (as jack must revel where he has been). but started to follow it unbeknown in the wrong direction back to the crime scene allowing me  to make my getaway that round. this game can make you sweat as you listen to the detectives talk and deduce... often giving you the impression they are in your head and know everything!!!

RATING: 7.5
I really enjoy this hidden movement game. It plays very differently from Scotland Yard, which, while fun, can drag a bit. The pre-kill bluffing/planning phases add a nice element to the gameplay, and the multiple night phases allow for some really interesting strategy (spend a couple of nights finding the trail, then extrapolate and start trying to catch Jack). The game rules come with balancing variants that can handicap both Jack and the Police and make it easy to balance for various player strengths.

RATING: 6
Whitechapel is a spiced up longer and more challenging Scotland Yard. At least as far as I can remember as I haven't played Scotland Yard for more than 20 years. Jack is out for four nights where he repeatedly has to escape to his hideout after his ugly deeds. While it is more difficult for detectives to locate and chase him than in its predecessor, information about his hideout is carried forward each night allowing them to draw the net closer around Jack. With AP prone groups Whitechapel can seriously drag. Four nights will be too long in that case. But in the right constellation, and especially for the Jack player it's a thematic and gripping experience. Contrary to the majority opinion, I prefer three players to only two players as listening to the investigators' discussions is a big part of Jack's fun in the game.

Reminder: Play that no Wretched can be killed twice even if she moved from the starting spot and got killed on another spot which - according to the rule - still would allow to use this starting spot in the next nights.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The game wears its theme lightly. The ghastly murders are off-stage, and the game is all about the hunt for the killer. The rules are well written and illustrated, easy to grasp and to remember, so the players are free to concentrate on the game rather than the mechanics. The board is complex and the possible routes that Jack could take are so varied that each game is different from any other. The atmosphere of the game is terrific - on the one hand the confusion and frustration of the police, and on the other the tension and the close calls as the noose closes.

The components - the beautifully designed board, the chunky pawns and the subtle counters are exactly to purpose.

The game is hugely entertaining. The police are in constant discussion and speculation, while Jack sits quietly, listening and hoping that the deductions lead to exactly this move, and above all not that one. Jack’s moves are typically quite fast, but one weakness of the game is that the police may take forever to move - an egg timer may come in handy. A second slight shortcoming is that it is really important that Jack doesn’t give a clue to his whereabouts by gazing at the bits of the board that interest him most. A third is that it’s a long game, taking perhaps 3 hours - though the game is exciting enough that people are prepared to risk missing their bus home…

One important piece of process for Jack: it’s worth having a short printed checklist for Jack’s coach moves: (1) note down both circles, in order, on the move track; (2) place the coach token on the time track and move the Jack pawn to the far end of the token. (It is only too easy to forget to mark both circles on the move track, and this can be crucial if Jack later claims not to have been in a circle because it wasn’t noted on the pad.)

Two important remarks:

(1) “Adjacent” has slightly different meanings that are crucial to the game - in particular, the balance is thrown entirely off if “adjacent” is misunderstood in the context of search and arrest.
Jack’s move: a circle is adjacent to another if it is joined to another by a dotted line that does not pass through another circle. Even though three intersections lie between them, 26 and 79 are adjacent for Jack.
Wretched move: as for Jack.
Wretched end of move: the Wretched may not end its move adjacent to a Police Patrol token. The Wretched may move from 27 to 44 if there is a police patrol at the square closest to 79, but not if there is one at the square between 44 and 46.
Police patrol look for clues or arrest: in a circle joined to their present position by a dotted line that does not go through a square. Police at the square immediately north of 79 may search in 79 but not in 44, 46 or 27.

(2) Most dotted lines meet at squares, but some intersections are clearly offset from a square, such as 44-26 and 65-83. there are also 6 intersections are more subtly offset. Thus there is only a single square between 45 and 46, and between 46 and 47. Similar offset crossings link 44-59, 30-50, 53-68, and 139-164, and there are two squares between 175 and 188 in either direction.

Two important house rules to balance the game: 
(1) The location of Jack’s hideout is chosen at random, either using an app on a mobile phone or by reference to specially-prepared random number tables. 
(2) Time starts at Roman V and Jack may not declare he is home before time reaches Arabic 1.

RATING: 9.5

So happy this got a reprint shortly after the con, it was an insta-purchase for me.

RATING: 9
Even if it is purely logic game the theme is very strong and perfectly matches the games mechanism. Great thematic euro game!

Comment after 19 (edit: new games added) games, out of which 13 were 2-players games and 6 were 4-players games.
Most of 2-players games (10 out of 13) ended up with Jack victory while none of 4 players games was successful for Jack. There were only two players in control of Jack equally sharing across the games. Seems that this is a psychological phenomena. While single inspector tends to lock into wrong paths, many inspectors, even if having disadvantage of discussing their plans in front of Jack, are able to think of more possibilities and are able to double check the deductions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The board game Letters from Whitechapel, which plays in 90-150 minutes, takes the players right there. One player plays Jack the Ripper, and his goal is to take five victims before being caught. The other players are police detectives who must cooperate to catch Jack the Ripper before the end of the game. The game board represents the Whitechapel area at the time of Jack the Ripper and is marked with 199 numbered circles linked together by dotted lines. During play, Jack the Ripper, the Policemen, and the Wretched are moved along the dotted lines that represent Whitechapel's streets. Jack the Ripper moves stealthily between numbered circles, while policemen move on their patrols between crossings, and the Wretched wander alone between the numbered circles.

RATING: 7.5
After one play, which I won as Jack, I am eager to play this game again. I was close to being caught several times but managed to slip away through pure luck or the investigators convincing one another to make different moves. There was some confusion among the investigators as to what had happened during earlier turns in the game ("We're sure Jack was at this spot during Night 1 and he moved normal, normal, carriage, alley, home, so his hideout is in this range" "No wait, wasn't it one normal then alley then carriage?" "Where even were the police during that night?") So some system of notekeeping for the investigators seems like it would cause less confusion and make for a quicker game, but then again maybe it would then be too difficult for Jack to win.

Edit 2/19/13: Having now played 4 times, twice as Jack and twice as an investigator, I feel like I now have a pretty good handle on this game. In my opinion, it is much easier to play as Jack, as he has perfect information (can see exactly where the police are, and in a lot of cases hear what they are planning as they discuss their strategy with one another). Both of my plays as Jack resulted in victories for me. My first play as an investigator was also a victory- that is, we caught Jack before the end of the 4th night. In that instance, I felt like our capturing Jack was due to luck more than skill. It felt like there was a lot of "well...maybe he went this way, I don't know, I guess we can try, oh hey look we were right."

My second play as an investigator was both aggravating and confusing. Although I was keeping notes, which I thought would help even the odds for the investigators, I spent much of the time just staring at the board and having no idea. The investigators ended up narrowing down Jack's hideout to one of about 4 or 5 potential spots in a small area, but we were never able to determine with certainty where he was going. In the end, the game had devolved into the investigators just checking the exact same spots over and over while Jack durdled around, and we decided to say "we think we have your hideout surrounded and we aren't moving for the rest of the 4th night. If you can't get home, we win, otherwise you win." It turned out we were off by one space and Jack was able to get home. This particular game lasted over seven hours, which certainly contributed to the general feeling of tediousness and frustration we felt at the end. Previous plays lasted about 3 to 4 hours.

I have yet to play as a 2-player game, which seems interesting for the investigators, or with any of the advanced rules, which are designed to either help Jack with one set of rules, or help the investigators with the other set.

If you are going to play this game, I recommend that you commit to play fast, at least on the investigators side, because it is very easy to get bogged down in trying to exactly determine Jack's whereabouts and remember all your information. Playing quickly means it is still possible to narrow down the location of Jack's hideout, and means you only have to spend the latest portion of the game truly puzzling out exact clues.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Letters from Whitechapel is a great game but it's not for everyone. In this hidden movement deduction game one player is playing Jack the Ripper. Every round Jack kills either one or two victims and then stealthily tries to get back to his hideout before the constables have him cornered or arrested. The other players (1 to 5) control the five constables who are out to catch him. As in most hidden movement games, one player's moves are hidden. He doesn't move a pawn on the board, but writes his or her movements down on a special notecard. The constables need to find them by moving their pieces on the board and investigating locations to pick up his trail.

I played this game after first having played Specter Ops, a game I absolutely love. But since I now own both and they're both hidden movement games I can't help but compare the two. 

I love Specter Ops but haven't played it as a four or five player game. The game scales differently with different player numbers and it can get problematic with a four or five player game. Also many of the rules aren't very clear. As a two or three player game it is magnificent however. 

Letters from Whitechapel plays equally well with all player counts. Regardless of the number of players, the game is always played with 5 constables versus Jack. In a 6 player game, one controls Jack, the other five players control one constable each. In a two player game, one player is Jack and the other player controls five constables. In a three to five player game some players control more than one constable. This balances the game far better.

My main gripe with Letters from Whitechapel is the playing time. I do enjoy long games if there's a lot to do and think about it. But Letters can take up to two or three hours and depending on AP prone players even longer. If you're playing with four, five or six players this can mean lengthy discussions and a lot of waiting time for the player who controls Jack. Also the game can suffer from boardgame dictators who'll overrule the opinions of less assertive players. 

A single game can suffer from less eventuful stretches where the constables have completely lost Jack for multiple turns. But the game can turn on a dime and suddenly become extremely tense and exciting. And when the game hits on all cillinders, it can be one of the most memorable boardgame experiences you'll ever have. 

But the game has proven devisive in my player groups. Some players love the deduction element, others absolutely do not. Make sure players know what they're getting into when you get started. 

Sold in december '17. Just too long. I prefer Specter Ops over this.

RATING: 7
This 1 vs all deduction game has rather simple mechanics that I think would make it very accessible yet allows for some clever bluffing. The different dot system for Jack's and the cops movement is something I haven't seen before. The thing is even if the player controlling Jack the Ripper has a clear path to their hideout, they don't necessarily want to just make a beeline towards there, as if a detective catches wind of the general location they are heading that can expose Jack's hideout in future turns. Instead, double backing to give false positives on the direction Jack is traveling seems to be key, although the Jack player must also not dawdle so long that they run out of time or allow the cops to find the actual path he took. 

I like the secret unit deployment in this game, as it makes sense that the cops would have a sense of where Jack would struck but not exactly where, and vice-versa. However, I find it strange that Jack has more time to escape the longer he dawdles.

RATING: 8
I enjoy Scotland Yard, and found Letters from Whitechapel to be an excellent variation on the theme.  I like the fact that it is more focused and iterative -- there is more focus due to the need to travel to the hideout, and it's more iterative in deduction due to the repetition over multiple rounds.  A wonderful deduction game that I always enjoy to play.  The theme is a harder sell than the more family friendly/neutral of Scotland Yard, especially with some players, but is actually very well done and fits like a glove with the mechanics of the game.

Personally, having played both Letters from Whitechapel and its newer/smaller sibling Whitehall Mystery I prefer Whitehall Mystery and no longer feel the desire to request Letters from Whitechapel unless there are more than 4 players.  Both games are good, but they are so similar that I would rather go with Whitehall Mystery since to me it feels tighter/more focused/has a more interesting map and is shorter.  If you have not played both siblings it's worth trying both since your personal preferences may be different.

RATING: 10
An excellent cat and mouse board game. It might seem complicated at first, but it's actually really simple to setup and play. Each stage of the setup is logical too. When playing as the policemen, the game feels very tensed, especially when the clues lead to dead ends. When playing as Jack, each turn is truly nail-biting, and it persists until the end of the game. As this game has no luck factor involved, whoever wins is truly the more skillful player and the other party respects that. For players who do not like to wait, this game cannot fully show its potential. For those who do not mind spending time to plan and plot, this game is a masterpiece.
