RATING: 9
This is a great game in so many ways. The rules are a thing of beauty, totally clear and unambiguous. The components are amazing -- this is one of my all-time favorite boards for any game.

As Jack, I feel the tension every turn, and it mounts steadily; lots of close calls. As a detective, it's fun to plan and play cat-and-mouse. It's a different experience with more than two players -- more collaborative, more tense in some ways as Jack. Two-player is sublime.

I also feel the theme, which was fantastic (and I love the theme itself). The board design is a big part of that, with long routes on the sparsely populated outskirts of the district and short hops in the tightly packed alleys -- the design really matches the theme beautifully.

The detectives should avoid writing anything down at all costs. Just talk it out -- building a spreadsheet to track things just bogs the game down, and Jack's turns are almost always going to be quick, thereby boring everyone at the table if the detectives take forever. This applies in two-player games as well.

Reports of this game taking too long are, I suspect, due to doing it "wrong." I've played as the police not writing anything down, and games played that way can take less than an hour and be a blast.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Rating based on 2 & 6 players.

While not particularly complicated, this game is very heavy and requires concentration--if you play games for light-hearted fun, this is not the one to play. However, the sense of tension is very good.

I wouldn't want to play this game with more than one investigator if I was on that side, though. You really have to coordinate movements in order to capture Jack, and in a group there is almost certainly someone who is going to alpha it, even if they don't mean to. When it comes to this type of game, I like being the boss!

-----------------------------------------

After playing the game (as Jack, and failing miserably) with 5 investigators, I can see the appeal of playing on a team (though it can make things much tougher for Jack!). I still think it's best as a 1-on-1 game, but if you've got a good group for co-ops, there is a lot of opportunity for teamwork.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Letters is just a masterful game. The most glaring problem with hidden movement games is balance. If one side is more powerful than the other, the game just falls apart, and it's all anyone can think of when playing the game. Letters does a great job of balancing the two sides, AND offering a bunch of variants you can throw in to make one side more powerful if your gaming group is having Jack or the police win more often. They even include extra components for some of these variants, which is a great touch. They realize that every group plays differently, so either Jack or the police will naturally begin having the advantage. If you're a fan of hidden movement games, this is a definite "must own", and if you haven't tried them yet, this is also a great one to test the waters in. It's a pure hidden movement game, no extra game mechanics thrown on top.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Rating after 1 play: 

I played this as a 2 player with my wife last night. I was Jack and she controlled the detectives. Playing Jack, there is a definite tension to this game. You feel as if you are being swarmed by the police, and you have to do whatever you can to divert them from your hideout. The components are fantastic, though I did have a slight quality control issue with my copy. Nevertheless, I think this is a great game so far.

Update: After a couple more plays, I have to say, I'm slightly disappointed in this game. As Jack, it's fun/semi nerve-wracking to hear the detectives debate about your hideout. So, Jack is fun, not exhilarating, but fun. Being the detective, on the other hand, can be quite dull. Even if you are on Jack's tail, he can screw with detectives so much (because of his movement abilities) that it becomes frustrating when the trail becomes cold. There's also a high degree of luck in the game, which is odd for a middle-weight deduction game. This luck factor comes at the beginning of each turn. If you make the "right" guess early in the round, you are more likely to be on Jack's tail, and force the game into, what I believe it was supposed to be: a race between the detectives and Jack to get to Jack's hideout. However, if you guess wrong (which is usually a 50/50 probability), you (the detective) will be cold the whole round, and it feels like your just going through the motions. There are literally no interesting decisions to be made if you are not on Jack's trail. All you can do is further speculate about Jack's path/current location/hideout. The game just falls flat at that point. Don't get me wrong, I LOVE being a detective in games. This is why I bought the game--because I enjoy deduction. I also don't mind a good challenge. The problem is the game feels quite unbalanced in Jack's favor, even with good detective work. 

I'll still play the game. I like the components, the theme, and the fact that it's cooperative. However, it won't be high on my list. Luckily, my wife enjoys it more than I do. Otherwise, it might be on my trade list.

RATING: 5
This is an extremely mediocre hidden movement game. It's very simple with very little complexity, but the game takes way freaking long. 

One night can easily last an hour with a full complement of players, and finishing the entire game can take 4 hours if Jack wins. The enjoyment I get from it isn't worth that four hour investment, especially when I can play Escape from Aliens in 10-30 minutes and have a far more tense, enjoyable experience, or just bring out Specter Ops and Fury of Dracula which are similar in mechanics but far superior.

Playing Specter Ops really highlights the problems with this game: it's rare to get that perfect balance between the hunter and the hunted that results in a close game.  In Specter Ops, the variable hunter powers almost always ensures that the game is close, as the Hunters will generally have a good idea of where you are and will only be a step or two behind, unless the Agent is exceptionally skilled and the Hunters are inexperienced. In White Chapel, either you find Jack's Trail or you don't, it's very easy to throw people off, and the Detectives don't have any abilities whatsoever and it can really drag for the detectives if Jack is well-hidden.

The game also fails to scale at all. With more players the game is heavily prone to quarterbacking, and adding players doesn't really do anything but lengthen the game as the mechanics don't change or scale at all. It just adds bland table talk as it's rare to get into heated discussions in this game.

RATING: 9.2
A tense and exciting hidden movement game that plays great with 2 players but is at its best with 3 players.  This is a really elegant and streamlined game of cat-and-mouse where the rules don't get in the way of the interesting game dynamics -- the chase!  

Jack has really difficult decisions to make each turn and is subject to a tremendous amount of game tension throughout.  Getting home each night is always euphoric and extremely satisfying.  

The police often find themselves lost in a fog of confusion.  After loosing the trail, finding a clue again elicits cheers and high-fives.  It feels like a wonderful achievement.

For both sides, this game creates an exciting and engaging experience throughout.  This is a great, great game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5

Be advised that the Nexus edition, at least, has some serious potential for exploitation by Jack.  Some rules tweaks and a gentleman's agreement can solve it, though.

RATING: 6
First edition...

Whitechapel is dripping with theme and the components are great! The designers have spent a lot of effort to recreate the experience of hunting a serial killer through the streets of 19th century London.

The mechanics work well, though Jack really has to know the rules. We had some games where Jack won because he moved in ways he wasn't really supposed to. When you realize thatafter about 2-3 hours of play, that can be frustrating.

The game is a very intense experience for both sides. We found out that if one player on the police side keeps a protocol of your investigation, Jack has a very hard time.

Whitechapel works best with 2 or 3 players in my experience. You can play with 5, but the game will last forever, downtime will increase and people will get tired after 2 hours.

RATING: 9

An "alpha player" can also ruin the experience for his/her fellow detectives. That can make the game long and taxing (even for Jack). So, think things through, but don't go overboard.

RATING: 7
I'm looking forward to the second edition of this. As I've read more it becomes increasingly interesting to me and i love hidden movement mechanics.
____________________
Update: played it and liked it. I feel like there is some tweaking needed to make it easier for detectives. I like the idea of making it that in any given night, Jack can't backtrack this way there are more clues to follow.  A really fun, no-randomness memory plus deduction plus sneaking game. Very fun, a bit of a brain-burner and won't always be in the mood. Definitely better than scotland yard without much added to the learning curb. Probably better than Ninja legend of scorpian clan though I like the implementation of that theme better. Overall, probably the best hidden movement game played so far and still tight.

UPDATE: Some variants are musts for this game. Allowing players to rush seems like a definite (rush meaning move again isntead of doing an investigation) also area arrests are a good variant. 

Another way to make it harder for Jack and also increase the dynamic nature of the path is make it so that Jack is not allowed to double back unless he has no choice or give him a limit to the number of times he can double back in a single turn.

RATING: 7.25
I was really hesitant to buy this at first, but after reading a few reviews (and really intrigued by the theme) I decided to take the plunge.  

I haven't played anything other than a 2 player game, and the tension is just right.  All of our games have been between 60 and 90 minutes.  I don't see any balance issues, but I firmly believe that the investigators need to use Area Arrests.

I've played both Jack and the investigators and I do have more fun playing Jack.  However, the investigators role is fun and challenging, too.  I would be curious to know if multiple people playing the investigators would take away from the overall experience.  Since I'm not a big fan of co-op games, I'm sure I wouldn't like this game as much.

Overall I think this game shines with 2 players.

RATING: 8
Our Rating Guide (1 = Disappointed, 3= Hit & Miss, 5= Owned it!)

1. Originality: 5 - Between the beauty of the components, the integration of historical detail, and the fact that this is the first game I've played where I really liked how players play different games, I'm a huge fan.

2. Artwork: 5 - when we first opened the box, all I kept saying is how beautiful the game is. Alright, the pawns and tokens aren't that lovely, they're pretty basic, but the details in the board, the tiles, the letters, even the rule book just astound me.

3. Quality of Contents: 4 - I have the revised edition, so I've been told that the quality of pawns and tokens has increased. They are functional and intuitive. Once again, the beauty of the game really wins for me.

4. Storyline: 5 - It's pretty simple, one person commits the murders, the rest try to catch him. The intensity of getting away or of hunting down your man really comes out in the game play. I love how the game tries to match onto the historical facts, but it's not following so close a storyline that the play becomes predictable. 

5. Playthrough: 5 - It didn't take too long to get the hang of it, and while I have so far only played with 2 players, the game continued to be exciting. 

6. Instructions: 4 - pretty clear for the most part, but of course it did take a few rounds to really get everything into place. 

7. Replayability: 5 - we played 3 full games back-to-back in our first go, so it definitely doesn't feel like it's going to get old any time soon. Adding to the fact that you can switch your role each time you play (being Jack the Ripper or an Investigator), you can really change the type of brain power you'll be investing in your game. I personally prefer being Jack the Ripper because I'm not that great at retaining information, whereas my husband and pretty much retrace my invisible steps just by calculating how many steps I've taken, so he can find me very quickly.

8. Two player: the game play stays the same regardless of number of players, so a 2 player game has no real disadvantage other than limiting opportunities to talk things out with another investigator. That being said, such talk can just end up being more helpful to Jack the Ripper so one player being all 5 Investigators can take more control over the Investigation.

9. Large Group: plays 2-6, and while we haven't tried it out yet, I can imagine some of the differences involved. So, with more than one Investigator, first-player gets chosen randomly. First player gets the job of placing all of the patrol tokens. So, if all of your Investigators communicate and are in agreement and follow through with all of their plans, then you'll have no issues. But I can already think of a few people that I would rather not play this game with simply because sharing control is not everyone's strong suit. Things could get very ugly if the one person made First Player decides to go against what the group agreed upon.

10. Fun Factor - 4. It's a thinking game so it's not going to be an unwinding game. I also feel far more stressed being the only Investigator than I do being Jack the Ripper, or, as I imagine, being a part of a larger group of Investigators who can perhaps keep track of things better than I can. But it's a good stressed, and I might get better with practice. I should also note that we haven't played with any of the variants yet. I'll adjust the review when we start adding these components in.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
So, after having Perry's copy of the game for 4 months, I got to play a round of Letters, playing my first game as Jack. And comparing this to Specter Ops, I think it's a totally different style of game. While Specter Ops is all about the powers, betrayal (with five players) and deception, Letters has the feel of gathering information across multiple rounds/nights.

It honestly feels like a puzzle that is manipulated by the Jack player. He determines where the detectives go, where they search and where to lead them to believe his hideout is. Instead of a hunt (Specter Ops), Letters feels like a chase/race. Jack has to time his getaway correctly as well. He can wait and make it seem as if he is still running around or he can end a round quickly and confuse the cops.

It's hard to tell who has the advantage between the two sides. But I definitely enjoy the game and find hidden movement as more than likely my favorite board game 
mechanic.

Initial Review: 8.75

Edit: I'm starting to see some of the problems with Whitechapel. It does not scale well. The more detectives there are, the more opportunities there are for one or more to become disengaged. And just as big of a problem: it's very long for what it is. At times, I feel like it's a thematic abstract game, so 2-3 hours (which can happen) is a lot. Still really like the game, but I have to drop my score.

RATING: 3
Reviewing Letters From Whitechapel is a difficult thing to do as the play experience as Jack is so different from that of the police. A game as Jack is a tense game of cat-and-mouse and worthy of LfW's high rank on BGG. A game as the police is a frustrating, repetitive affair that one despairs of even by mid-game and is a major turn-off. The game can be very long or anticlimactically short, neither of which is good.

I like cooperative games, competitive games, and partnership games, but the imbalance of the 1 versus everyone else game play I find distasteful. Add to that the serial killer theme and I find very little about LfW to recommend.

Plus, the game's 'Waiting To Kill' phase is illogical: The movement of the Wretched allows for murders to occur in the same area multiple times, and the waiting gives Jack more time to escape rather than less.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Letters is a highly thematic "fox-and-geese" game similar to Scotland Yard or Fury of Dracula. One player controls Jack the Ripper; selecting a victim and announcing the scene of crime, while the other 1-4 players work together as a team of police, searching and attempting to catch Jack before he escapes to his lair. Movement is point-to-point, although Jack has some special movement options that he can only use once or twice each night. There are four rounds: on each "night" Jack has fewer victims to choose between, and hopefully the detectives have begun to work out where his lair is. The game becomes very tense, and quite exciting.

RATING: 7.5
規則:
*偵探永遠使用五個(小於五人則有人多控)，拿對應參考表，並洗勻偵探領袖標記疊起，拿走所有偵探棋子跟受害者棋子
*傑克拿移動記錄紙跟屏幕，放一個棋子在夜晚記錄軌第一格，並決定他的藏身處並拿取所有對應標記跟參考表
*遊戲共經過四夜，每夜分成兩個階段，只要傑克都成功脫逃回藏身處，傑克獲勝，反之偵探獲勝
**階段一 地獄**
1.傑克拿取對應此夜的特殊移動標記(3C2A/2C2A/2C1A/1C1A)
2.傑克拿取對應此夜的女人標記(All/7-4m/6-3m/4-1m)並蓋著放在圖板上紅色數字區(必須沒有犯罪標記)，有標記m的代表可能的目標
3.偵探打開領袖標記決定此回合的領袖，領袖蓋著放置七個警探標記在版圖上的黃邊黑點上，有顏色的對應之後的偵探，第二夜開始，上回合偵探在的五個位置一定要放(顏色可不一樣)，剩下放在黃點上
(傑克在此時可以使用信件功能)
4.傑克翻開所有女人標記並換上受害者棋子，時間標記放在下面羅馬數字1位置上
5.傑克選擇殺一個目標則把一個女人棋子換成犯罪標記進入8.或是 等待(時間標記到5時就不能再等)並進入6.
6. 時間標記左移一格，偵探領袖移動所有女子棋子一格(不能重疊)且不能停在跟偵探標記相鄰位置或跨過偵探標記，或是停留在有犯罪標記處
7.傑克可翻開一個偵探標記，如果是假的移除，然後回到步驟5.
8.傑克選完人殺後，標記在自己紙上，對應夜跟時間標記位置上標示數字，然後把第二個傑克棋子放在時間軌上(第三夜兩個數字分別填兩格，靠右格會起始地點)
9.偵探打改所有偵探標記並置換對應偵探棋子，也移除剩下的受害棋子
**階段二 追捕**
1.傑克移動一格數字並記錄在紙上同行右邊的空格，不能跨過偵探棋子，並移動圖板上的時間標記右邊一格，不能動直接算輸，如果移動到藏身處直接宣告並結束此階段進入下一夜，行動時間到底也直接輸，可是用特殊移動標記，使用後放在對應的時間標記上，回藏身處的移動不能用特殊移動
> 馬車 一次花兩個時間移動兩格，且可以跨過偵探，且一定要移動，不能回溯
> 小巷 可跨過城市到相鄰城區的另一個數字格
2.由偵探領袖開始輪流移動自己的偵探0~2格(黑點上)，可跨過偵探但不能停在同格
3.每個偵探由領袖開始順時鐘對周圍數字格動作二選一，階段沒結束就回1.
>找尋線索 可以對周圍數字一直問直到出現一個線索停止或全部問完，如果傑克該夜有經過過就放一個線索(特殊規則 當放上5個線索時，傑克可以拿一個假線索標記)
>執行逮補 宣告一個相鄰的數字，如果傑克在上面就獲勝，不然就沒事
*如果傑克逃脫，階段二結束，所有偵探跟犯罪標記留在場上，線索移除，傑克棋子移動到下一晚，並回到階段一
**** 額外規則 幫助傑克
*傑克的信 第二夜開始，每夜可是用一封傑克信來改變偵探標記位置
*錯誤線索 在第二階段3.時傑克可放置在任何一格數字格上，該格不能被找尋線索
**** 額外規則 幫助偵探
*奔跑 多一個行動為可幫其他尚未移動過的偵探棋子移動
*範圍逮補 改為直接逮捕周遭的所有數字格
*快來抓我  傑克不能選擇紅色數字跟相鄰格為藏身地
*我知道你的位置 如果決定偵探領袖時為棕色或紅色，可馬上執行額外逮捕行動，宣告任一個數字格，如果是傑克藏身處直接獲勝，一場遊戲只能用一次

RATING: 7.5
Thematically, this is one of my favorite games; and the board and its components are gorgeous. This one-versus-all, hidden movement game is very tense and always a thrill to play, no matter what side you're on or how large the group is. I remember leaping into the air with joy when I found a clue or my heart pounding with fear when investigators came close to my hideout. Fraught with tension and anxiety, Letters from Whitechapel is an immersive dive into the world of Victorian London. Time between player's turns and AP can be an issue but I hardly noticed the time passing.

RATING: 7
This game is fantastic, if a little long. The asymmetry of the two side and the tensions that it creates are up there with the best. I really like the theme and have no issue with playing Jack, like some seem to on BGG; I feel that the theme gives the police real incentive.

The turn structure can look daunting when viewed on paper, but in reality this game runs along as smooth as you can imagine. The majority of the game consists of the chase after each killing and it is this phase that makes the game so enthralling. The player taking on Jack's role can spend the entire time filled with tension, hoping that the police won't watch on while he weaves his way through the London streets towards his hideout and the police spend much of their time totally baffled at how the clues have created the path that they have, often thinking that Jack is long gone, when he is really only one or two steps ahead.

I only wish that the game was a little shorter. As Jack in particular the experience can be mentally exhausting; one that is worth it though.

RATING: 10
This game is a lot of fun! It's easy to learn and teach. New players can jump right into the action their first play-through which makes this a perfect game for when you have those friends over you don't see on a regular basis swing by for game night. 

Playing as Jack and playing as the investigators provides 2 completely different experiences. You will definitely feel pressure when playing as Jack. It's fun to hear what the investigators are talking about. There has been quite a few times where they were going to make an arrest on the spot I am in but decided to choose a different location. You need a good poker face. Close calls like those are what makes the game so thrilling. 

The only downside is once you really get to know the game as Jack, you can easily outsmart the investigators. You need to add some variants in to help out the investigators or you will almost always escape. I have yet to be caught as Jack but always have close calls during the game so for me, it's still as exciting as my first play through.

Okazja
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Very engrossing game. One player plays Jack the Ripper, the other(s) play the detectives and vigilantes trying to catch him. The game is played over a beautifully rendered map of the Whitchapel neighbourhood and the goals are simple: each murder night (there are 4 in total), Jack has 15 turns to get from the murder scene to his hideout. If he fails to do so in any of these nights - whether by being caught or by running out of time, he loses the game.

THe mechanics are very simple. The map consists of a series of circles (used exclusively by Jack) and dots (used by the police players). After choosing a victim,  Jack registers his moves on a grid hidden from the other players and, each turn (plus a few that allow him to move two circles or cross a block. These looking for clues (i.e. if Jack passed through a certain circle) or declaring an arrest in circles adjacent to the dot the detective's pawn is in.
What is great about this game is that it is fun not only for the player playing Jack but also for the others. For Jack, it's about teasing the police and get to the hideout in the right time: follow an excessively direct route and you will reveal the whereabouts of the hideout too easily; follow an excessively tortuous rout and you risk running out of turns to get there. For the police players is about and methodically closing down the circle on Jack and restricting his possible moves.   
Highly recommended, but be ready for some 2+ hours of play if the game goes all the way.

Edit: I tired from the game after a few more plays. It's assymetrical in play and also on the fun factor: great if you play Jack, not so much if you're the Detectives. In a game of 2+ hours, it is two much.

RATING: 8
Sometimes I feel guilty over giving so many of my games a rating of 8 or better. Then I realize it's because I try not to keep games in my collection that I'm not very impressed with or excited to get repeated plays from. I don't want a huge board game collection filled with a bunch of 4's and 5's.

Whitechapel is a confident 8, and a game I know I'm going to want to hold on to. 

From the stellar FFG components and graphics, to the dark and twisted theme. Simple to learn. It's a clever design. Everything makes sense and creates a game where the tension is always mounting. There are moments where you actually gasp when you realize the killer has slipped right through you fingers, that he had just been [i]right[/i] there next to you.

It scales down to 2-player very well, plays fast once you get the hang of it, and so far feels pretty balanced. 

There are no dice rolls, no cards draws, There seems to be no element of 'luck' at all. The way to win is to out-think you're opponent. It calls on you to be cunning, brave and just a little bit twisted. 

It's really unlike anything in my collection of pretty great games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
A compelling game. I've added victim pictures, a timer to rush the detectives (a bit), and a mini-maglight for the head investigator to "point" things out on the map; all these add a bit of atmosphere.

We also use the "Todd" rule, which allows detectives to run (move 3) if they don't search. This helps pull players back into the game if the have been assigned to the outskirts at the beginning of turns. 

I'd like to hear more about other house rules.

RATING: 9.12
This game was just simply fantastic. The rules are simple enough to learn and the game play is fantastic. A 1 vs many hidden movement game as Jack makes his killings and tries to get back to his hideout in each of the 4 nights. The 5 investigators are searching for clues to track Jack's whereabouts, locate his hideout, and make an arrest. This game was very thematic and suspenseful. As Jack you really feel as if you're being hunted. It's not boring with AP prone people, but it can double the length of the game. Also, need to be careful of one person on the investigator side taking over and quarterbacking the entire time. Its likely not on purpose. The game just really sucks people in.

RATING: 8
Klassisches, und nicht nur meines Erachtens ‚das‘ klassische Deduktionsspiel um „Jack the Ripper“ für 2-6, eigentlich aber für 4-6 Personen. Zentraler Mechanismus: „Jack“ muß in bestimmten Nächten, innerhalb eines bestimmten Zeitfensters Morde begehen (hierfür stehen unterschiedliche Opfer ‚zur Verfügung‘) und dann zurückkehren zu einem anfangs von ihm (geheim) festgelegten Versteck. Hieran sollten ihn die Polizisten hindern. Der Spielplan von London ist so gestaltet, daß sich Jack und die Polizisten nie (!) auf einem Feld begegnen können, sie können nur einander benachbart sein (was den Polizisten jedoch für die angestrebte ‚Verhaftung‘ reicht). Höchst spannend, einfache Regeln (die das Spiel indes positiv vom guten, alten „Scotland Yard“ absetzen). Und es gibt ‚Blutspuren‘.

RATING: 5
It doesn't really improve on the hidden movement experience from childhood favourite Scotland Yard.

I don't like how you "restart" the hunt over and over, and the clumsy mechanics, that is used, if Jack doesn't kill right away makes no sence neither in the game nor thematically.

A cynical Jack player can win the games in 15 minutes, not leaving the police officers a chance.

Even if you use a random hideout generator, for a game that is almost as long as Fury of Dracula, this lacks flavour and depth. It's tense but mostly for the Jack player.

Some might prefer a more "pure" hidden movement experience than Fury of Dracula, but I would go for a shorter and simpler game than this one.

RATING: 6
I see this as a 2P or perhaps 3P game only as more Policemen wouldn't be very fun to play.  3P is interesting as each police player has some interaction and Jack can hear some of the dialogue on plans and what the thoughts are.

I have seen the variant of allowing Police to move without performing an action, but suggest it be ignored for beginning players.  This super-move does allow the police to close the net very quickly and the Jack player needs to be astute to avoid getting ensnared - something that likely comes with experience in the game.

A nice deduction game with great tension on both sides.  Jack as he attempts to bluff and mislead his way back to his hideout, the police as they try to close the noose tighter and tighter around Jack.  Careful attention to the board and forethought on moves required on both sides.

RATING: 7
13-06-2015. Rated after 1 play. In the game, one player plays Jack the Ripper, murdering wretches over several nights. The other players are police trying to catch Jack.

Each night, you have several potential wretches you can kill as Jack. The Police gets to draw the wretches closer every time you don't kill a wretch. You have to kill one in a couple of turns, but you can manipulate when.

Once you kill a wretch, you are at the location of the wretch. You then are allowed to move to a hidden location one space away, or two spaces away if you use a carriage or to an unconnected location within the same area. You then run away towards your at the start selected hide-out.

The agents try to find you, by asking at locations if they can see you. With that, they try to hunt you down and arrest you before you go back home.

I like the game, but it's slow and thinky. It's good, but really works best with a low player count and it MAY be too serious for me. Regardless, it works fine and if you enjoy those kind of games it might just be it for you.

RATING: 3
This game is awful. Given the topic and the indeed fantastic effort that the authors put in the art and in all the included material about Jack and it's history, you would expect a game made with the exact same dedication. What you instead have is a sloppy clone of Scotland Yard with the pace of a slug that crawls with a limp. Really, the only player that can really have an almost sadistic fun with this game is the one running Jack (at least until he/she realize he would have to wait tons of minute every turn while the investigators try to make up their mind about their moves), or the investigators if Jack is playing bad. There is really no middle ground about that. Even if the skill level of both sides is equal, it's still boils down to an endless stream of "are you there?" "no" "are you there?" "no" "are you there?" "no" and so on. The only way investigators can try to win is by guess, given that they know where Jack was at step one, where he may have passed at step three of four, because go for the easiest path (find step two) will easily allow Jack to cover more and more distance in the meanwhile, therefore giving away nigh to 0 clues about the location of his hideout.

There is really nothing much else to say. Fury of Dracula, which is surely not the perfect game too, is a way more engaging and interesting Scotland Yard-like game. This one is recommended only for those ones who really likes the idea of breaking their minds on a simple and pure "where is the other guy" problem.

RATING: 3
1 play, 3er

I was Jack, and it was sort of fun at first. Tense, certainly. But we quit after 2 rounds ... I can't imagine doing this over and over again! 

Were you at 79?
No
78?
No
80?
Yes

Wow, that's some fun play right there!

--- Summary ---
One player is Jack. The others control the 5 detective pawns in the game. The board is a map of Whitechapel, with streets creating blocks. There are stops on all the streets, different for investigators and for Jack/victims. At the start of the game, Jack picks a location as his secret hideout. 

Each day (of 5!), Jack places the potential victims and, over the next several rounds, decides which one to kill. Then, he starts to make his getaway, recording his every step on a secret sheet one by one (may use a carriage or alleyway a limited 0-2times, depending on which round it is, to take multiple steps or a shortcut). At the same time, the other players move their detectives and search for clues: when they stop moving, they ask Jack if he was at an adjacent location (may keep asking until they hear "yes"). If they think he's there now, they can try to capture him instead of investigating (if successful, they win; otherwise, Jack does).

If Jack makes it to his hideout, the day ends and you repeat the above. The detectives can shift the position of 2 of their pawns, and Jack cannot use a starting location where he's already killed. And on one of the days, there are 2 murders instead (with detectives getting to move in between). Otherwise, though, you repeat the above until all the murders are complete and Jack makes it home again, or until Jack is found.

I kind of like the hidden movement mechanism, but it felt really repetitive and boring despite the tension of having the detectives SO close to Jack many times! Scotland Yard might me more to my liking -- from what I hear, it has similar mechanics but plays much more quickly?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Hidden movement/chase games just aren't for me.  I loathed Nuns on the Run and didn't care for Scotland Yard.  For me, too, this game is nearly a Scotland Yard clone but with an extra 2 hours of play time tacked on.  No thank you.

Each round begins with a bit of setup/preparation.  Jack the Ripper can buy himself more time during the actual chase phase of the round while also learning the locations of investigators while the investigators can move the target pawns into better positions.  Honestly though, very little is gained this way and it just feels like more time added to a game that already feels too long for what it is.

Then the chase half of a round feels exactly like Scotland Yard but changed for the worse in a way that makes it take so much longer.  Jack moves secretly every round and then the investigators move and search for Jack on surrounding locations or attempt to make an arrest.  In this way each investigator has to name off surrounding locations one at a time (since you can only find one clue on a turn) looking for clues.  Jack still has special move tokens but he doesn't have to reveal his location when he uses one in this game.  In order to make an arrest you don't simply need to wind up on Jack's space either, you need to make an arrest at his exact location.

Also, this goes on for 4 rounds.  Just too long and a clone of a game I already didn't particularly enjoy.  I will say that it all works well together and if this is the type of game you would enjoy I think it is a great entry into the genre.  It's just not for me and I certainly am not going to spend this much time playing a game that doesn't resonate with me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Superb deduction game: one player takes Jack the Ripper and the other is the police hunting him down. Jack moves secretly but the police can block intersections and search for clues along his trail. Components are gorgeous. It's basically a gamer's version of Scotland Yard with an extremely grim theme.

One of the most charming aspects is how thematic it all feels. Even many seemingly "gamey" tactics, such as the cops indiscriminately arresting passersby, are perfectly in the spirit: in the real investigation over 80 suspects were detained.

It's a wonderful game of cat-and-mouse... albeit one where it does not pay to dwell on the details of what precisely is being simulated.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Really nice, neat, easy to play deductive game. For me personally it probably falls short of a 9 or a 10 purely because of the lack of variety in strategy, the very simple mechanic of checking squares and deducing Jack's path is almost the entire game. Having said that though it's good at what it does, after 10+ plays I've found it to be well balanced, and I like the beautiful little Jack and investigator figures.

One additional point on the cooperative side of the game: I don't think there's enough of a co-operative side to this as the investigators. I really enjoyed playing with other people just because we could chat about ideas and it really felt like an investigation team trying to catch the criminal. That having been said the actual game itself didn't really mechanically add anything for having one or multiple investigators even though it was fun both ways.

RATING: 10
As at the time of writing these comments I have had 5 games, all 3 player. Jack (emma on this occasion) has won once. Other games went to the police. However, gameplay does not feel as one sided as this might suggest. I have played police every time so far and there are times when you feel the desperation of things slipping away from you and that you will never be able to catch jack and then.... You pick up a clue.... And another nearby and suddenly the chase is back on and adrenalin pumps! This is a fantastic game that has jumped to the top of my favourite 'long games' list overnight. The deduction can feel overwhelming (so many branching routes that jack could have taken) but a well thought out and methodical search will help - although on its own will not be enough. You need to also attempt to get into the rippers head and figure out what he might have done if you want to stay on the trail. Catching him up, getting ahead and arresting are not easy and require the narrowing down of the likely hideout address and routes to and from. It feels like a real achievement when you finally slap the cuffs on him! I really love the game. However, I can see why some may not. Firstly, it's really not easy to track down jack and some may find this frustrating. Secondly, amongst the police it is very easy for one player to dominate the others to the point where their involvement is negligible. Third, jack can have a really long wait in between turns while the police figure out what to do. Lastly, if you want to win as police you are going to need to take pretty good notes and spend some time reviewing them. None of the above bothers me and this is a new favourite at the time of these comments (7/9/14)

RATING: 8.5
This is a really cool game group vs. one deduction game, basically a stripped down, streamlined version of Fury of Dracula. There's no combat mechanic, just a pure chase. There's a couple of problems with this: the hunt can become a little tedious, especially for Jack, and the game can feel repetitive if you play it too often. However, the rules are extremely easy to learn, the set-up is simple, and the game is just beautiful. With two players or a group of experienced players, it doesn't feel overly long (however, groups can be prone to lots of talking and can stretch the length a little, but I think a game which gets people talking is a good thing if you don't mind a longer game). It can be VERY intense at times and has a great edge-of-your-seat appeal towards the final turns. It's definitely a thinker, though, so you'll be taking lots of notes. It always leaves me feeling intellectually satisfied.

RATING: 10
Very fun strategy game of deduction in the same mould as one of my favourite games, Fury of Dracula.  Whereas FoD is mostly a chase game with plenty of chrome and wild swings of luck, this is a pure chase game with no luck whatsoever.

I was wondering if it would feel too abstract since it's so streamlined.  Not at all.  This game is highly thematic, and reminded me very much of a scene in a movie in which the cops are poring over a pin-map, trying to find the pattern that will lead to capture.

I also think this would be a far better choice if you only have 2 players.  FoD has too much stuff to administer.

UPDATE: I think I may prefer this to FoD...

RATING: 7
The game seems completely broken in favor of Jack if you play with the rules as written. If Jack's hideout is next to or within two spaces of one of the wretched, then on rounds three and four he can just kill and go straight home before the police have a chance to act.

Still, if Jack doesn't use that strategy (and possibly with the help of some houserules) this is a better version of Scotland Yard. In this game the detectives can ask for clues about where Jack has traveled, and they win by attempting to arrest Jack at his current spot. This is a little more interesting than Scotland Yard where you just try to land on the same space as Mister X.

Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 5
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 3


Overall - 1

RATING: 8
This is a terrific game. A friend of mine has a copy and after playing it once I just had to have a copy of my own. I've played it with 3-4 players so far and I can see why it'd be best as a 2 player game. Playing one policeman is a bit boring because there's little for a single policeman to do. You move and either gather clues or try for an arrest. That's it. If playing all 5 policemen then you make all the decisions. You either win or lose by your own deductive reasoning and the police aren't being divided up unevenly between players if played with fewer than the maximum of 6.
The rules are generally well written. The artwork and materials are of good quality although there are some intersections on the board that have black policemen squares questionably placed. Also, with just 5 policemen it's terribly difficult for them if they can't locate Jack's hideout. But then Jack never was caught so thematically making it balanced in Jack's favour makes the game wonderfully challenging.
Overall it's a simple yet addictive game of cat and mouse. I give it an 8.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Letters from Whitechapel is a fantastically evocative game of true deduction and pursuit. The mechanics of the game are minimal and simple, which leave for a true player vs. player feel. The absence of random draws and event cards allows for the players to feel that they are playing one another and not having the challenge artificially "enhanced" by the game itself. It's dark themes are not overstated in the components, but the mood set by the play still may draw them out too much for some players, making this game an uneasy fit for most families or many new gamers. However, if you are not put off by the theme, this truly is a masterpiece. 

Full review at: http://boardgamegeek.com/article/6568674
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Pretty cool game. This is NOT a reworking of Scottland Yard or Fury of Dracula. That'd be like saying all deck building games are reworkings of Dominion because it was the first. No this is a game that uses the system and makes a game all it's own. Much like Garibaldi before this one was it's own game. This game was a lot of fun.

UPDATE as suspected this is best as a 2 player game. First outing was a full 6 but now with a 2 player game down this is where the game shines.

RATING: 6.5
[thing=59959][/thing] is a game much easier to learn than I expected. Basically Jack kills a victim and then has 15 turns to reach his refuge. The policeman need to find Jack's tracks and either arrest him or prevent him from reaching his hiding place. Jack moves through the numbered spots in the board, while the policemen move up to two spots by the square spots, and once they all did their movements, they can ask, one at a time, for the Jack player if he passed by any of the number connected to the squares spots the policeman are at. If Jack did, it ends the turn of the policeman asking (so, if this happen in a place with three numbered spots connected, and Jack passed by the first place the policeman asked, the policeman player can't ask more about the other places), and it is put a yellow marker in the number to show that Jack passed there. So, using the yellow markers, the policemen try to judge the path of Jack, considering the distance he moved, and where he can be.

Jack has some tricks to use: first, he can backtrack; also, he has two things that help his movement: carriages (move twice in a turn) and lanterns (cross a block - meaning that Jack doesn't need to follow the path in the board, but can "jump" from a number to other, but this one must be connected to the same block). Also, in the set up of the round, Jack position the markers for the possible victims, and the police don't know which is "true" and which is "false" - but also the chief of police (one elected player) position the police in such a way that it also has some "false" policeman, but Jack can check these markers to see if they are or aren't false by delaying the kill, and is able to look at one marker per turn he delays, however the police can then move the victims markers closer to the policemen, which might make the fleeing after the assassination harder. This all could be seem as a difficult task for the policemen and that Jack has the upperhand, but the experiences with the game show something else: that Jack usually is caught somewhat easily.

The Jack player only wins if he avoids the police for four rounds, killing and returning to his refuge all the times. The police have four rounds to find his hideout and prevent him from reaching this place, or arrest him while he is on the move.

I considered [thing=59959][/thing] harder for Jack than for the police - but we did play with one rule wrong: after the movement of a cop, the player already asked about the position of Jack, so the next cop could take this into consideration and plan his movement accordingly, and this made the game for Jack much harder. Even with this, if if I compare it with its sibling, [thing=20963][/thing], in which Dracula has a lot of resources, not only to stay hidden, but also many servants, powers and events, while Jack basically only has one advantage: he can backtrack, and this can throw the police off. The carriage is akin to Dracula's wolf form and bat form; and Jack's lantern reminds me of the sea travel, as Dracula can reach several places without following a given path.

Now, for those that don't like the luck factor present in [thing=20963][/thing] (sometimes in the movement; mostly there in the combat; and also in the events and encounters), but really enjoy the "hunt", [thing=59959][/thing] is the answer to your wishes. There is no luck here besides that which comes from the unpredictability of the humans beings - every now and then the most logic course of action would be do X, but the player will do Y, which appear of nowhere and make almost no sense, and will hit gold. In [thing=59959][/thing] there aren't events nor dice. Jack's movement and those of the policemen are always chosen by the players, inside their limitations.

However, it is hard to ponder about any unbalance in the game, because even when Jack is caught early, usually he is a few steps away to leaving the grasp of the police - it is usually a matter of a lucky move, or one turn more (** and more plays later showed that, with all the proper rules, Jack can be tough to be found, and Jack had two straight wins, the second a really easy one **). So I can see that the dispute can be a head to head knife fight, but, until now, such a play didn't happened - which is probably due to Jack's player requiring more savyy and being a more difficult to play than that of Dracula in [thing=20963][/thing], therefore experience on his side have even more weight.

The game bits are nice, thematic and the overall graphic design is pretty great.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Get ready to enter into the poor and dreary Whitechapel district in the London 1888, the scenario of the mysterious Jack The Ripper murders, with its crowded and smelly alleys, hawkers, shouting merchants, dirty children covered in rags who run through the crowd and beg for money, and prostitutes - called "the wretched" -- on every street corner. The board game Letters from Whitechapel will take the players right there: one player plays Jack the Ripper, and his goal is to take five victims before being caught. The rest of the players - 1 to 5 - are police detectives, who must cooperate to catch Jack the Ripper before the end of the game.

RATING: 9
The best deduction game. It's not even close. I've heard the complaint that it's dry, but whereas Fury of Dracula has a great theme and components, Letters is the better experience and design. As Jack, the player toys with the investigators when it suits their strategy and flat out evades them when needed for survival. The game can be anywhere from 40 minutes to 3 hours (depending on the amount of deliberation and how clever/quick Jack is or is not). Complaints of balance are best addressed by adding optional rules from the book as needed. I play with two very different groups. Group A are investigators that track every guess and clue, so they prefer scaling back area clues (limited to lead investigator each evening) and catch an experienced Jack 50% of the time. Group B allows area clues for all investigators and assigns Jack a random hideout. Jack escapes more than 50% of games. I think that balance will depend on the group.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The best deduction/chase game hands down, and one of my favorite games period.  The 3+ hours it takes feels like just one as players are constantly on the edge of their seats. Playing Jack and the investigators are two completely different experiences, which makes it almost like two different games. 

Our investigators use laminated maps and dry erase markers for assistance. For those seeking an intense battle of nerves and wits, and who enjoy both deduction and spatial games, you should be in heaven.

I use the following variants:

a) Investigators may move a third space in lieu of searching for clues or making an arrest, what is now officially known as the "rushing" variant.

b) If Jack walks home on the first move of night 4, Investigators get to take their turn.

c) Jack's hideout is random.

It took me all 15 turns to get back to home base for night one.  Night two went much quicker as  the murder was close to home.  The detectives were sure they knew where my base was, but they were so wrong and Night three was easy and four went just as quickly.  So much pressure in night one that the rest seemed like a breeze.  I sure enjoyed playing as Jack; will see how I feel as a detective.

RATING: 5
Not sure what the hoopla is about.  Playing [i]Whitechapel[/i] is like being forced to squirm through four straight games of Scotland Yard, which itself is only modestly entertaining.

I'd never want to play a full game of LfW.  For me, two rounds is more than enough.  There's only one thing to do on your hunter turn, and that's move and clue-hunt.  Do that 500 times, and you'll have some idea as to the one-dimensionality of the whole affair.

And if you play Jack, be prepared to say "no" a lot.  

[i]Hunters: Are you at 65?

Jack: No.

Hunters: 72?

Jack: No.

Hunters: 75?

Jack: Wait...maybe...let me check.  OK, still no.[/i]

RATING: 8.175
First time we played, Jack lost pretty early in the game. The second time another player of my group totally fooled us and was able to get away. It's a good hidden movement game and depending on Jack can be so hard for the detectives to figure out where he went, but that also makes the game very long sometimes. If the detectives or Jack have to think about their turns for too long it can get boring for the "opponent". Although as Jack I always think about future turns during the detectives turns. I sometimes have a bit problems with the mini map, because you still have to check where the detectives are on the map, and you don't want to give away too much with where you're looking at.
In the end I really enjoy the game even if it drags on at times. My game group likes it too, so we play it occasionally.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Just one play, a long evening at a friend's place. Took us about 3 hours, I was Jack. I must say, I can't remember almost anything of what happened during that much time.
I was sweating, I was thinking... for three hours that felt like a minute and during the final two longest player moves of my life, was I really Jack the Ripper?

Can't tell, honestly. This game made me go back to board games... actually it made me discover them, seriously. Just one game with it, so I can't really give a 10 here as it wouldn't be fair (it needs more plays), but boy, it was the most intense game I had that I can remember!

Update (Aug 2012): after repeated plays, I'm starting to see the reasons behind the complaints about LfW. It's true, everything is totally engaging on Jack's side, but on the investigator team things differ quite strongly depending on who is playing. This may result in better or worse experiences, but ultimately is more a flaw in the semi-coop genre than a fault of the game itself.
I'm decreasing the score down to 8 also because I feel this is a game that doesn't benefit from frequent plays, as it feels a bit "seamy", especially for the investigators.

Update (Dec 2012): impressive fall from grace! Due to the continuous request of this game in my group, I'm starting to heavily dislike it. It's still a solid offering in the genre, but it will now need a good amount of "rest" before my desire to play it again recovers.

RATING: 8.5
Unas reglas sencillas y sublimes que hacen que los calores, nervios y la excitación se manifiesten en todos los participantes. 

Jack vuelve a casa con las manos manchadas de sangre haciendo repiquetear con orgullo sus pasos en la noche londinense. El acoso y el debate que los policías tejen a cada paso que da el asesino no tiene precio. 

Los inspectores se abalanzan frustrados confundiendo al criminal con los transeúntes, tratando de adivinar su paradero a toda velocidad. Cuando Jack llegue a su guarida  estará a salvo. Londres no. Cuatro noches. Cinco asesinatos en sus calles.

Un juego que no deja de salir a mesa.

RATING: 3
I could see how this might be fun if you're Jack the Ripper, but as a cop, this was a total snoozefest. I've never felt so disinterested in a game. The play time is way too long for what it is. I'd rather play pretty much anything else.

Specifics, as a cop, your job is to poke around mindlessly until you find out where Jack was several turns ago. It takes several full rounds to get an educated guess at where Jack's hideout is, which could be at least an hour. I'll admit that once we were pretty sure we knew where Jack's hideout was, we could more easily discern his movement path because he had to hang around it, the game got a little more interesting, but before that point, I just wanted my character to help Jack kill his victims so the game would end faster.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
